Waste Recovery Enters. LLC v. Town of Unadilla


90477


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


294 A.D.2d 766; 742 N.Y.S.2d 715; 2002 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5384



May 23, 2002, Decided  
May 23, 2002, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal dismissed by Waste Recovery Enters., LLC v. Town of Unadilla, 100 N.Y.2d 614, 799 N.E.2d 618, 2003 N.Y. LEXIS 2548, 767 N.Y.S.2d 395 (2003)
Appeal denied by Waste Recovery Enters., LLC v. Town of Unadilla, 1 N.Y.3d 507, 2004 N.Y. LEXIS 92 (2004)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Waste Recovery Enters. v. Unadilla, 2004 U.S. LEXIS 4199 (U.S., June 14, 2004)



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff company and defendant town cross appealed from an order of the Supreme Court (New York), which, inter alia, partially granted the company's cross motion for summary judgment and declared Unadilla, N.Y., Local Laws Nos. 1 (1989), 4 (1992), to be unconstitutional. However, the trial court modified Unadilla, N.Y. No. 1 (1988), by severing a provision, and enjoined the company from operating the landfill.


OVERVIEW: The company had applied to the department of environmental conservation (DEC) for permits to build and operate a solid waste transfer station and a construction and demolition debris landfill in the town. After receiving the permits, it constructed the transfer station and landfill. Thereafter, the town notified DEC of two laws affecting plaintiff's proposed operations resulting in a series of claims and counterclaims by the parties. The appellate court held a reading of the 1988 law made plain that the town board, had it foreseen that a section would be declared unconstitutional, would have intended the enforcement of the nonoffending portion. There was no assertion that the company's representatives made specific inquiry regarding any statutes affecting the construction and/or operation of a landfill in the town or that they made any efforts to ascertain the existence of any laws or regulations with regard thereto. Therefore, the company could not assert estoppel.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

People v. Miles


590, KA 99-05522


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


294 A.D.2d 930; 741 N.Y.S.2d 774; 2002 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4535



May 3, 2002, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury in the Supreme Court, Erie County (New York) convicted defendant of sexual conduct against a child in the first degree and endangering the welfare of a child. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court held that the trial court did not err in refusing to excuse for cause a juror who worked for the department of corrections and who stated that most prison inmates were guilty. The juror indicated that nothing about his experience prevented him from being fair and impartial, and thus defendant failed to show that the juror's state of mind was likely to preclude him from rendering an impartial verdict based upon the evidence. The appellate court held that expert testimony regarding abused child syndrome or similar conditions was properly admitted to explain behavior of a victim that might have appeared unusual or that jurors may not have understood. There was no requirement that lesser included offenses be available for every crime. Defendant failed to preserve for review his further contentions. The evidence, the law and the circumstances of the case, viewed in totality and as of the time of the representation, established that defendant received meaningful representation. The evidence was sufficient to support the conviction, the verdict was not contrary to the weight of the evidence, and the sentence was not unduly harsh or severe.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

678 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


In re Stone


266, 267


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


294 A.D.2d 59; 740 N.Y.S.2d 335; 2002 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4083



April 25, 2002, Decided  
April 25, 2002, Entered



DISPOSITION: Orders entered 10/20/2000 and 11/15/2000 reversed without costs; applications were granted; the courts were directed to conduct hearings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Consolidated appeals from the Supreme Court, Bronx County (New York), required the court to examine certain provisions of the Insanity Defense Reform Act of 1980, specifically N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 330.20. The lower court determined that it lacked jurisdiction and discharged respondent insanity acquittees without any inquiry as to their mental condition or need for further supervision.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court found that a court had to conduct a hearing to determine whether there was good cause to extend the orders of conditions under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 330.20(1)(o), or whether the insanity acquittees meet the criteria for discharge pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 330.20(13). Both insanity acquitees' psychiatrists submitted affidavits setting forth sufficient allegations of potential danger to warrant hearings. The interests of public safety were not be ignored by allowing the insanity acquittees to be released, unsupervised into the community, without first determining whether they were in need of further observation to protect themselves and the public. Accordingly, the orders which dismissed applications to extend orders of conditions pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 330.20(12) were reversed, on the law, without costs, the applications granted, and the courts directed to conduct hearings to determine whether there was good cause to extend the orders of conditions or to determine whether the insanity acquittees meet the criteria for discharge.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the lower courts, ordered the applications to extend orders of conditions granted, and the lower courts were directed to conduct hearings.


HEADNOTES

Mental Health - Person Acquitted of Crime by Reason of Insanity - Extension of Order of Conditions after Expiration Date

The failure to hold a hearing before the expiration of an order of conditions imposed on an insanity acquittee under the supervision of the State Office of Mental Health pursuant to CPL 330.20 does not divest the convicting court of jurisdiction and require that acquittee be discharged. Nothing in the statute expressly precludes a court from addressing an extension of an order of conditions after its expiration date, particularly where, as here, "good cause" supports the application. Rather, the statutory scheme requires that a court conduct a hearing to determine whether there is "good cause" to extend an order of conditions under CPL 330.20 (1) (o), or whether an insanity acquittee meets the criteria for discharge pursuant to CPL 330.20 (13). Dismissing a petition to extend an order of conditions as untimely on jurisdictional grounds is improper. The policy in favor of continued supervision of insanity acquittees prior to discharge, combined with a broader concern of protecting the general public from releasing potentially dangerously ill patients, unsupervised, necessitates disregarding a mere technical defect, and also warrants a broad view of the court's jurisdiction to extend an order of conditions. The interests of public safety should not be ignored by allowing insanity acquittees to be released, unsupervised into the community, without first determining whether they are in need of further observation to protect themselves and the public.

 

679 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Sheffield Towers Rehab. & Health Care Ctr. v. Novello


2001-01595


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


293 A.D.2d 182; 741 N.Y.S.2d 103; 2002 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4004


September 6, 2001, Argued  
April 22, 2002, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed in part; motion for preliminary injunction denied; defendants' cross motion to dismiss third cause of action granted; matter remitted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent health center submitted to appellant department of health (DOH), an application for the construction of a nursing home. After the DOH notified the center that the application was subject to a moratorium, the center sought a preliminary injunction to enjoin the DOH from applying the moratorium to the application. The Supreme Court, Nassau County, New York, granted the center's motion from which the DOH appealed.


OVERVIEW: The trial court granted the injunction on the ground that the moratorium was inapplicable to the center's project as it violated N.Y. Pub. Health Law art. 28. The appellate court held that the center did not make a prima facie showing that the DOH violated or threatened to violate any provision of N.Y. Pub. Health Law art. 28. Thus, the center did not demonstrate a likelihood of success on the merits ad was not entitled to a preliminary injunction. Because of the uncertainty regarding anticipated demand for nursing home beds, the DOH properly exercised its discretion to delay a decision on the pending applications in order to study public need. The declaration of a moratorium was within the health commissioner's inherent discretionary power and was consistent with the proper exercise of a statutory duty only to authorize construction of necessary facilities. The moratorium was not a rule or change of practice, but was rather a temporary suspension of the processing of applications. There being no violation of N.Y. Pub. Health Law art. 28, the trial court should not have enjoined the DOH from discharging its responsibility pursuant to N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 2802.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reversed.


HEADNOTES

Health - Nursing Homes - Validity of Temporary Moratorium on Processing Conditionally Approved Nursing Home Projects

Defendant Department of Health (DOH) acted within its statutory authority in determining that plaintiffs' conditionally approved application to construct a nursing home was subject to the DOH's statewide temporary moratorium on the processing of nursing home projects pending a review on the needs for nursing homes throughout the state. Supreme Court erred in preliminarily enjoining the DOH from including plaintiffs' project in the moratorium and directing defendants to continue processing plaintiffs' application. The DOH has a statutory duty to approve the establishment and construction of a nursing home only where it is convinced that there is a public need therefor (Public Health Law § 2802; see also § 2801-a [3]). Because of the uncertainty regarding anticipated demand for nursing home beds, the DOH properly exercised its discretion to delay a decision on the pending applications in order to study public need. The conditional and contingent approval of plaintiffs' project was not tantamount to final approval of the certificate of need application. Consequently, when the temporary moratorium was imposed, plaintiffs' application was pending and subject to the moratorium. Moreover, the temporary moratorium was not a rule or change of practice, but was rather a temporary suspension of the processing of applications. A temporary suspension, imposed for a valid and reasonable purpose in keeping with the agencies' responsibilities under the statute, is within the authority of the DOH so long as its duration is confined to that reasonable period of time needed to review and revise the public need methodology.

People v. Montgomery


11854, 12787


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


293 A.D.2d 773; 742 N.Y.S.2d 126; 2002 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3417



April 4, 2002, Decided  
April 4, 2002, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended August 8, 2002.



DISPOSITION: Judgment and order reversed; motion to vacate granted; indictment dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from two judgments of the Supreme Court of Albany County (New York) that convicted defendant of the crime of criminal possession of a controlled substance in the third degree, and denied defendant's motion pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 440.10 to vacate the judgment of conviction.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, defendant contended that he was entitled to have his conviction vacated on the basis that his defense counsel's failure to seek a determination that his arrest was not supported by reasonable cause to believe that he had committed a crime. Defendant also contended that his attorney failed to have his statement to the police suppressed as the product of an illegal arrest. The appellate court found that, from its review of this record, the appellate court could discern no legitimate strategic or tactical explanation for defense counsel's failure to seek a Dunaway hearing. Further, in the absence of some information known to the police that singled out defendant as the one who possessed the crack cocaine, his warrantless arrest lacked probable cause. Moreover, the only admissible testimony pertaining to defendant's statement found in the record pertained solely to statements made postarrest, and did not provide the necessary probable cause for defendant's arrest. Nor did the appellate court find that defendant's later oral statement admitting ownership of the crack cocaine excused the lack of probable cause for his arrest.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed; the motion to vacate was granted, and the indictment was dismissed.

 

681 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hall


329 KA 99-05424


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


291 A.D.2d 143; 738 N.Y.S.2d 782; 2002 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2677



March 15, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by People v. Hall, 98 N.Y.2d 651, 772 N.E.2d 613, 2002 N.Y. LEXIS 2085, 745 N.Y.S.2d 510 (2002)
Post-conviction relief denied at People v. Hall, 306 A.D.2d 957, 760 N.Y.S.2d 69, 2003 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6957 (N.Y. App. Div. 4th Dep't, 2003)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Motion denied by Hall v. Conway, 2008 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 48347 (W.D.N.Y., June 23, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to a plea bargain, defendant pleaded guilty to two counts each of murder in the first degree and murder in the second degree. The Supreme Court, Monroe County, New York, sentenced defendant to life imprisonment without parole on each count of murder in the first degree, to run concurrent to consecutive terms of 25 years to life on each count of murder in the second degree. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued on appeal that his pleas were invalid, as they was entered during the 120-day period provided for the prosecutor to elect to file a notice of intent to seek the death penalty pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 250.40(2). Defendant relied on the Matter of Hynes rule that a defendant could not plead guilty to first degree murder while a notice of intent was pending. The appellate court noted the qualification to that rule that plea bargaining to lesser offenses even when a notice of intent was pending, or to first degree murder in the absence of a notice of intent, was not affected by the rule. The plea agreement offered by the People to allow defendant to plead guilty in exchange for life in prison without parole was essentially an announcement of an intention not to seek the death penalty, such that his plea fell within the qualification to the rule and was valid. By pleading guilty, defendant waived his other contentions that his guilty pleas to first and second degree murder for the same offense violated the constitutional prohibition against double jeopardy and that the trial court erred in allowing the district attorney unrestricted subpoena power.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the judgment.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Plea of Guilty - Capital Murder - Validity of Plea Entered Prior to Expiration of 120-Day Period within Which People May Seek Death Penalty

1. Defendant validly entered a guilty plea to two counts each of murder in the first and second degrees (Penal Law § 125.27 [1]; § 125.25 [1]) prior to the expiration of the 120-day period within which the People could elect to seek the death penalty (see CPL 250.40 [2]) where the People had not filed a notice of intent. While a defendant may not plead guilty to first degree murder while a notice to seek the death penalty is "pending" pursuant to the decision of the Court of Appeals in Matter of Hynes v Tomei (92 NY2d 613, cert denied 527 US 1015), invalidating the plea provisions of CPL 220.10 (5) (e) and 220.30 (3) (b) (vii), the term "pending" cannot be interpreted to include the entire 120-day period, regardless of whether a notice of intent has in fact been filed. Here, the plea agreement offered by the People, allowing defendant to plead guilty in exchange for a sentence of life imprisonment without parole, was the functional equivalent of an announcement of an intention not to seek the death penalty. In the absence of a notice of intent, the plea bargaining at issue remains unaffected by the decision in Matter of Hynes v Tomei. In addition, defendant's remaining claims regarding the prosecutor's unrestricted subpoena power and an alleged double jeopardy violation were effectively waived by his plea of guilty.

Crimes - Plea of Guilty

2. In a prosecution for first degree murder defendant specifically waived his contention that the court erred in allowing the District Attorney unrestricted subpoena power. Even in the absence of that specific waiver, defendant effectively waived that contention by his plea of guilty.

Crimes - Former Jeopardy

3. In a prosecution for first and second degree murder, defendant's comprehensive waiver of "the right to appeal the conviction on all grounds" encompasses his contention that the constitutional prohibition against double jeopardy was violated because he pleaded guilty to first and second degree murder for the same offense. In any event, the constitutional prohibition against double jeopardy bars successive prosecutions or punishments for the same criminal offense, not the simultaneous prosecution of a crime and its lesser included offenses, and therefore is inapplicable to defendant's contention.

Mallinckrodt Med. v. Assessor of Argyle


90537


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


292 A.D.2d 721; 740 N.Y.S.2d 467; 2002 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2462



March 14, 2002, Decided  
March 14, 2002, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended August 21, 2002.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner medical corporation appealed the judgment of the Washington County Supreme Court (New York), which dismissed the corporation's application, in a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law art. 7, to reduce a real property tax assessment on certain real property owned by the corporation.


OVERVIEW: The corporation and respondent town assessor stipulated a settlement of a proceeding brought to review the assessed value of the corporation's property. Later, the corporation sought a reduction in its tax assessment, claiming overvaluation. The trial court granted the assessor's motion to dismiss, finding the proceeding barred by N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 727. On appeal, the corporation argued the current stipulated valuation violated N.Y. Const. art. XVI, § 2, which prohibited tax assessment valuations in excess of full value. The appellate court noted that by executing the stipulation, the corporation waived any constitutional protection afforded under the New York Constitution. However, the appellate court determined, if it was to reach the merits of the corporation's constitutional arguments, it would find each to be lacking. The appellate court reasoned the statute had a rational basis, sought to address and remedy a circumstance detrimental to the public welfare, and sought to achieve a balance between the competing interests in the least restrictive manner.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the judgment.

Hertz v. Hertz


2001-00646


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


291 A.D.2d 91; 738 N.Y.S.2d 62; 2002 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2161


December 3, 2001, Argued  
February 25, 2002, Decided



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remitted for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner grandfather filed an action, pursuant to N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 72, seeking an order granting him visitation with his grandchildren. In response, the grandchildren's parents made a pre-answer motion to dismiss the grandfather's petition. The Supreme Court, Kings County (New York), held that § 72 was unconstitutional, and it dismissed the petition. The grandfather appealed.


OVERVIEW: After a grandfather and his wife separated, the parents of his 15 grandchildren refused him permission to visit his grandchildren or to have a relationship with them, and the grandfather filed an action, pursuant to N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 72, to obtain visitation rights. The appellate court held that the United States Supreme Court's decision in Troxel v. Granville did not mandate a finding that § 72 was unconstitutional per se, and the trial court erred in dismissing the grandfather's petition on that ground.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the trial court's order, denied the parents' motion to dismiss the grandfather's petition, reinstated the grandfather's petition, and remitted the case to the trial court for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Parent and Child - Visitation Rights of Grandparents - Constitutionality of Domestic Relations Law § 72 

New York's grandparent visitation statute ( Domestic Relations Law § 72) is not facially invalid under the decision of the United States Supreme Court in Troxel v Granville (530 US 57, 147 L. Ed. 2d 49, 120 S. Ct. 2054). That decision held that a "broad" nonparental visitation statute was unconstitutional as applied because of the failure to accord any deference to a parent's decision regarding visitation. Troxel does not prohibit judicial intervention when a fit parent refuses visitation, but only requires that a court accord "some special weight to the parent's own determination" when applying a nonparental visitation statute. New York's statute has been interpreted to accord deference to a parent's decision, even though the statute does not specifically require such deference. Consequently, Supreme Court, in dismissing petitioner grandfather's application to obtain visitation with his minor grandchildren, erred in concluding that Domestic Relations Law § 72 is unconstitutional on its face and in dismissing the petition on that ground.

Boden v. Jackson-Silver (In re Boden)


1666, CAF 01-01634


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT

February 1, 2002, Decided

685 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Davidson


1664, KA 01-01306


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


291 A.D.2d 810; 737 N.Y.S.2d 467; 2002 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1087



February 1, 2002, Decided  
February 1, 2002, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The People appealed from an order of the Supreme Court, Monroe County (New York), which granted defendant's motion to set aside the verdict convicting him of loitering in a public place for the purpose of gambling with cards, dice, or other gambling paraphernalia, N.Y. Penal Law § 240.35(2). The trial court had determined the law was unconstitutional because it was indistinguishable from N.Y. Penal Law § 240.35(3), which was invalidated.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court held the issue was whether N.Y. Penal Law § 240.35(2) was reasonably related to the public good. The police had observed a group of several young men, including defendant, who were shooting dice for money in a residential driveway. The appellate court held that N.Y. Penal Law § 240.35(2) did not punish conduct anticipatory to constitutionally protected activity. Rather, it was enacted under the authority of the State's police power to prohibit loitering in a public place, not for the purpose of all gambling, but only for the purpose of gambling with cards, dice, or other gambling paraphernalia. That power was very broad and comprehensive and in its exercise the conduct of an individual and the use of property could be regulated so as to interfere, to some extent, with the freedom of the one and the enjoyment of the other. Thus, N.Y. Penal Law § 240.35(2) did not need to be invalidated merely because it regulated otherwise legal activity.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the trial court's order, denied defendant's motion, reinstated the verdict, and remitted the matter to the trial court for sentencing.

People v. Whitehurst


78842, 11331


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


291 A.D.2d 83; 737 N.Y.S.2d 152; 2002 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 861



January 31, 2002, Decided  
January 31, 2002, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by People v. Whitehurst, 98 N.Y.2d 642, 771 N.E.2d 844, 2002 N.Y. LEXIS 1845, 744 N.Y.S.2d 771 (2002)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Certificate of appealability denied Whitehurst v. Senkowski, 2007 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 29809 (N.D.N.Y, Apr. 23, 2007)



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the County Court of Ulster County (New York) which convicted him upon his plea of guilty of the crime of murder in the first degree. Defendant also appealed, by permission, the order which denied his motion, pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 440.10, to vacate the judgment of conviction.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued on appeal that his guilty plea had to be vacated because the trial court failed to specifically advise him prior to accepting the plea that he would be relinquishing his rights under a cooperation agreement and abandoning his pretrial motions to dismiss the indictment and suppress evidence, and that his guilty plea was the result of ineffective assistance by trial counsel. The appellate court found the plea was knowingly, intelligently, and voluntarily made and operated to extinguish any claims that defendant could have had under the cooperation agreement, as well as his rights to pursue his pretrial motions, despite the trial court's failure to explicitly address those specific consequences of the guilty plea. The appellate court also determined that considering the overwhelming evidence against defendant and the uncertainty of success on his pretrial motions, defendant received a favorable plea bargain. Therefore, under the totality of the circumstances viewed at the time of the representation, the appellate court concluded that defendant received meaningful representation.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the judgment and order.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Plea of Guilty - Vacatur - People's Repudiation of "Cooperation Agreement"

1. Defendant is not entitled to vacatur of his guilty plea to murder in the first degree for having killed a seven-year-old girl with a blow to the head with a rock after sexually abusing her. Defendant led law enforcement personnel to the girl's body in a wooded area buried under leaves and other debris after signing a written "cooperation agreement," whereby he agreed to disclose information relative to the child's whereabouts in return for the People's promise not to charge him with murder. However, the People expressly repudiated the agreement and charged defendant with murder after an autopsy and forensic examination established that, contrary to defendant's assertion when he signed the agreement, the child had died instantly as a result of a massive blunt force injury to the head. The record demonstrates that defendant's plea was knowingly, intelligently and voluntarily made, and operated to extinguish any claims that defendant may have had under the "cooperation agreement," as well as his rights to pursue his pretrial motions, despite the court's failure to explicitly address those specific consequences of the guilty plea. By pleading guilty, defendant forfeited his right to appellate review of any nonjurisdictional defect in the proceedings. Furthermore, the People's use and subsequent repudiation of the "cooperation agreement" in an attempt to locate a missing child does not constitute fraudulent activity which would warrant vacatur of defendant's plea. In any event, defendant's own material misrepresentation provides a basis for rescission.

Crimes - Right to Counsel - Effective Representation

2. In a criminal prosecution wherein defendant was convicted of first degree murder upon his plea of guilty after he led police to the body of his victim pursuant to a "cooperation agreement," defendant's argument that his guilty plea was the result of ineffective assistance by trial counsel is rejected. Considering the overwhelming evidence against defendant and the uncertainty of success on his pretrial motions, defendant received a favorable plea bargain. Moreover defendant expressly stated during his plea colloquy his satisfaction with the representation he had received.

Crimes - Plea of Guilty

3. In defendant's first degree murder prosecution wherein he pleaded guilty and received a sentence of life in prison without parole, his argument that his plea should be vacated because, subsequent to his plea, the Court of Appeals found the plea provisions of the death penalty statute to be unconstitutional (Matter of Hynes v Tomei, 92 NY2d 613), lacks merit. The Court of Appeals has since expressly held that the infirmity of the plea provisions does not, in and of itself, render invalid an otherwise valid guilty plea (People v Edwards, 96 NY2d 445). Since defendant's plea was otherwise knowing, voluntary and intelligent, that case is dispositive.

 

687 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


220 W. 98 Realty, L.L.C. v. N.Y. Province of the Soc'y of Jesus


5119


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


291 A.D.2d 13; 737 N.Y.S.2d 335; 2002 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 834



January 29, 2002, Decided  
January 29, 2002, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at 220 W. 98 Realty LLC v. N.Y. Province for Soc'y of Jesus, 2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1569 (N.Y. Civ. Ct., July 20, 2005)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landlord appealed and respondent Jesuit province and local Jesuit community cross-appealed, the affirmance by the Supreme Court, Appellate Term, First Department (New York), of the trial court's denial of both sides' motions for summary judgment in a holdover proceeding.


OVERVIEW: Years earlier, the landlord's predecessor had entered into a lease with a province of the Society of Jesus to rent a large number of apartments for use by priests and seminarians. After the seminary closed, the local Jesuit community continued to lease a smaller number of apartments for use by priests. The landlord commenced a summary holdover proceeding to end the community's rent-controlled tenancy, arguing that under case law interpreting New York's rent stabilization laws, a corporate tenant was not entitled to a renewal rent-controlled lease unless the lease specified a particular occupant of a particular apartment. The reviewing court agreed with an intermediate court that the local community had satisfied this condition years earlier when it supplied a list of occupants. To the extent that those occupants remained in their apartments, the community was entitled to a renewal lease as to those apartments.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the intermediate court's decision and remanded for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and Tenant - Rent Regulation - Renewal Lease - Apartments Used by Members of Religious Organization as Primary Residences

Respondent, a religious organization which rented apartments in petitioner's building for the residential use of its members, is entitled to rent-stabilized renewal leases for those apartments occupied by members identified as occupants in a 1989 stipulation which resulted in the execution of rent-stablized renewal leases for the specified apartments. A corporate tenant is entitled to a renewal lease under the Rent Stabilization Law so long as the lease specifies a particular individual as the occupant and no perpetual tenancy is possible. The parties' stipulation wherein the landlord specifically recognized that the specified apartments were the primary residences of the occupants satisfies that requirement. Since the stipulation identifies particular individuals as the occupants of specific apartments, no perpetual tenancy is possible. However, the Appellate Term properly remanded for a determination as to which apartments are no longer occupied by the individuals designated pursuant to the 1989 stipulation.

 

688 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Stokes


10934B


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


290 A.D.2d 71; 736 N.Y.S.2d 781; 2002 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 581



January 24, 2002, Decided  
January 24, 2002, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Writ of certiorari denied: Stokes v. New York, 2002 U.S. LEXIS 5747 (U.S. Oct. 7, 2002).



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the County Court of Chemung County (New York) upon a verdict convicting defendant of the crime of aggravated harassment of an employee by an inmate and sentencing him under the persistent felony statute.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court held that the testimony convincingly established that defendant, an inmate, threw a combination of urine and feces on a correction counselor, knowing she was a prison employee, with intent to harass her. Defendant was not entitled to a circumstantial evidence charge. It was not an abuse of discretion to permit defendant's legs to remain shackled during the trial. Defendant had previously been convicted of two or more felonies, and the trial court correctly concluded that defendant was a persistent felony offender. The trial court did not abuse its discretion in determining that defendant's history and character and the nature and circumstances of his criminal conduct indicated that extended incarceration and life-time supervision would best serve the public interest. The trial court afforded defendant ample opportunity to be heard, addressed defendant's individual history, and stated its reasons at length, emphasizing defendant's 20-year history of felonies, many of which involved violence, the planned nature of the present crime, which the court described as reprehensible and degrading, and the need to condemn and deter such conduct within prisons.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Aggravated Harassment of Employee by Inmate - Weight of Evidence

1. Defendant's conviction of aggravated harassment of an employee by an inmate ( Penal Law § 240.32) for having sprayed a liquid combination of urine and feces at a correction counselor from his prison cell was not contrary to the weight of evidence and should therefore be affirmed. Although the counselor, who had cited defendant in a misbehavior report a week before the incident, did not actually observe defendant projecting the substance at her, she testified that seconds before the incident she saw a sheet hanging in defendant's cell move to the side and then heard squirting sounds emanating from the cell, which was immediately adjacent to her and which defendant alone occupied in 23-hour lock down. Consequently, her testimony that the substance emanated from defendant's cell was neither incredible nor unworthy of belief. Moreover, while a subsequent search of defendant's cell approximately 15 minutes after the attack failed to reveal the presence of any of the sprayed urine/fecal substance or the container, if any, used to expel the substance, the jury could reasonably have concluded that the delay in the search afforded defendant ample opportunity to clean up and dispose of any incriminating evidence.

Crimes - Evidence - Circumstantial Evidence Charge - Aggravated Harassment of Correction Employee by Prison Inmate

2. Defendant, a prison inmate, was not entitled to a circumstantial evidence charge in his prosecution for aggravated harassment of an employee by an inmate ( Penal Law § 240.32) for having sprayed a liquid combination of urine and feces at a correction counselor from his prison cell, since there was direct evidence regarding defendant's sole presence in the cell from which the substance emanated. Although the counselor did not actually observe defendant projecting the substance at her, she testified that, seconds before the incident, she saw a sheet hanging in defendant's cell move to the side and then heard squirting sounds emanating from the cell, which was immediately adjacent to her and which defendant alone occupied in 23-hour lock down.

Crimes - Trial - Proper Use of Leg Restraints

3. In defendant's prosecution for aggravated harassment of an employee by an inmate ( Penal Law § 240.32) for having sprayed a liquid combination of urine and feces at a correction counselor from his prison cell, the trial court did not abuse its discretion in permitting defendant's legs to remain shackled during the trial. The decision was expressly based on security concerns attributable to defendant's criminal history, his inmate disciplinary record and the nature of the crime charged.

Crimes - Sentence - Persistent Felony Offender - Aggravated Harassment of Correction Employee by Prison Inmate

4. The imposition of a consecutive 15-year to life prison sentence under the persistent felony offender statute ( Penal Law §§ 70.10, 70.25 [2-a]; CPL 400.20) upon defendant's conviction of the class E felony of aggravated harassment of an employee by an inmate ( Penal Law § 240.32) for having sprayed a liquid combination of urine and feces at a correction counselor from his prison cell should not be set aside as being harsh and excessive. Defendant received the minimum authorized persistent felony offender sentence. The sentencing court did not abuse its discretion in determining that defendant's 20-year history of violent criminal conduct, his lengthy inmate disciplinary history, including assaults on prison staff, and the nature and circumstances of his criminal conduct warrant extended incarceration and life-time supervision under the persistent felony offender statute. Furthermore, the sentencing court properly relied on the need to deter such conduct by prison inmates in imposing a lengthy A-1 felony sentence.

Haubeck Realty Corp. v. Tax Appeals Tribunal


85330


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


289 A.D.2d 715; 734 N.Y.S.2d 313; 2001 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12000



December 13, 2001, Decided  
December 13, 2001, Entered



DISPOSITION: Tax tribunal determination confirmed without costs; petition dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayers appealed a determination of respondent New York State Tax Appeals Tribunal sustaining an assessment of real property transfer tax gains against them, pursuant to former N.Y. Tax Law art. 31-B. They argued the taxable value should have been reduced by long leases to which the property was subject.


OVERVIEW: Former N.Y. Tax Law art. 31-B imposed capital gains taxes on transferors of real property. The taxpayers sold some property while the law was in effect, but they objected to the amount of transfer tax assessed against them, arguing that the value of the gain realized should have been reduced by the value of the 99-year leases to which the property sold was subject. The court held that while former N.Y. Tax Law § 1440 did indicate that long leases should be treated as prior conveyances of real property, it only reduced taxable value where the prior conveyance resulted in an assessment of transfer tax. Since the leases predated the former tax's enactment, there were no taxes to recoup. Furthermore, the tax was not irrationally based, so it did not violate due process or equal protection guarantees.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the assessment decision.

 

690 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Seward Park Hous. Corp. v. Cohen


2600


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


287 A.D.2d 157; 734 N.Y.S.2d 42; 2001 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12140; 114 A.L.R.5th 789



December 13, 2001, Decided  
December 13, 2001, Entered



DISPOSITION: Appellate court order entered 2/25/2000 reversed without costs; petition dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landlord commenced a holdover summary proceeding to evict respondent tenant for harboring a dog. The trial court dismissed the petition, and entered a final judgment of possession in favor of the tenant. The landlord appealed, and the Supreme Court, First Department (New York) reversed and granted a final judgment of possession in the landlord's favor. The tenant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The parties' lease provided there were to be no pets. The tenant openly and notoriously harbored a pet for three months, with the actual knowledge of the servants and employees at the building, who were not required by the landlord to report the pet harboring. Under New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 27-2009.1, the failure to seek enforcement of a no-pet clause in a lease within three months resulted in the landlord's having waived that breach of the lease. In rejecting the landlord's argument that it did not have knowledge of the pet harboring, the highest court found that the three-month statute of limitations required routine awareness on the part of the landlord. The landlord would not be able to stop the running of the three-month period simply by closing its eyes and ears. In the instant case, the tenant routinely walked his dog three times a day, and everyone around saw it. The fact that the landlord's agents were not required to report it did not change the fact that their knowledge would be imputed to the landlord. The failure to commence the instant proceedings within three months was deemed to be a waiver of the no-pet provision of the lease.


OUTCOME: The appellate court's order was reversed, and the petition dismissed, with the final judgment of possession in favor of the tenant reinstated.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and Tenant - Harboring Animals in Leased Premises - Waiver of No-Pet Clause

In a holdover summary proceeding, petitioner landlord waived its right to enforce a no-pet covenant in respondents' cooperative proprietary lease where respondents "openly and notoriously" harbored a pet for three months with the actual knowledge of building personnel, and petitioner failed to commence a summary proceeding within that three-month period as required by Administrative Code of City of New York § 27-2009.1 (b) in order to enforce the covenant. The ordinance provides that where a "tenant ... openly and notoriously for a period of three months ... harbors ... a household pet ... and the owner or his or her agent has knowledge of this fact, and such owner fails within this three month period to commence a summary proceeding or action to enforce a lease provision prohibiting the keeping of such household pets, such lease provision shall be deemed waived." The ordinance was intended to remedy the abuses arising from the infrequent, selective and dilatory enforcement of no-pet clauses, and is applicable to all existing leases, including cooperative proprietary leases, excluding only buildings owned and managed by the New York City Housing Authority. Proof of "openly and notoriously" harboring the pet in the apartment for a period of three months or more raises a presumption of knowledge by the owner or the building's managing agent of this fact. The word "agent" should be broadly construed to include the maintenance staff, porters and security guards at respondents' building who had actual knowledge that respondents were harboring a dog in their apartment. Furthermore, the ordinance requires that the actual knowledge of such on-site building personnel be imputed to the building's non-resident managing agent, even if such employees were not expressly required to report tenant harboring of pets. A landlord may not avoid having imputed knowledge of the tenant harboring a pet by turning a "blind eye" to such an open and notorious fact.

People v. Collins


11865


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


288 A.D.2d 756; 733 N.Y.S.2d 289; 2001 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11525



November 29, 2001, Decided  
November 29, 2001, Entered



DISPOSITION: Trial court judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), upon a verdict convicting him of second-degree criminal mischief in an incident involving spraying of a solution containing chicken excrement on the front of the hall of the state's high court.


OVERVIEW: Defendant raised many constitutional challenges to his prosecution and conviction for criminal mischief in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 145.10 after admittedly spraying a loathsome solution all over the front of the hall of the Court of Appeals of New York. The court reviewing his conviction held that the record clearly contradicted his claims that he was denied the right to counsel at arraignment and before the grand jury, because he explicitly waived those rights, and he was known to have extensive prior contacts with the justice system. There was also plenty of evidence that the damage he caused fell within the statute's prohibition. The hall stank for months, and enormous expenditures were required to eliminate the stench. Finally, the statute was not unconstitutionally vague; it clearly described the proscribed conduct.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

692 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Flow v. Mark IV Constr. Co.


88004


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


288 A.D.2d 779; 733 N.Y.S.2d 751; 2001 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11534



November 29, 2001, Decided  
November 29, 2001, Entered



DISPOSITION: Compensation board decision affirmed without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Claimant employee was disqualified from receiving workers' compensation benefits. Thereafter, hearings were held to address the employee's activities subsequent to April 16, 1997, after which a workers' compensation law judge ruled that the employee was not fraudulently receiving benefits. Respondent New York Workers' Compensation Board reversed and disqualified the employee from further wage replacement, and the employee appealed.


OVERVIEW: The employee asserted that N.Y. Workers' Comp. Law § 114-a was unconstitutionally vague. The appellate court disagreed. The statute provided that a person would be disqualified from receiving compensation where he or she obtained benefits by knowingly making a false statement or representation as to a material fact. To the extent that the employee contended that the statute was unconstitutionally vague because it failed to define the term "material fact", the appellate court held there was no requirement that every term in a statute be precisely defined. Rather, a statute would pass constitutional muster so long as it provided persons of ordinary intellect reasonable notice of the proscribed conduct. The appellate court held the term in question could be understood by a person of ordinary intelligence and was thus not susceptible to arbitrary enforcement. Indeed, the challenged phrase had a common understanding and was used, without definition, in many other statutory schemes. As to an assertion that the statute was criminal in nature and that the substantial evidence standard of review could not apply, the appellate court had previously rejected such an assertion.


OUTCOME: The decision was affirmed, without costs.

Broadway Schenectady Entm't Inc. v. County of Schenectady


89352


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT

November 15, 2001, Decided  
November 15, 2001, Entered



DISPOSITION: Condemnation determination confirmed and petition dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. Em. Dom. Proc. Law § 207, petitioner lessee sought review of a determination of respondent county which condemned the lessee's property as the site for a community policing center.


OVERVIEW: A lessee intended to operate an adult bookstore on the property until the county voted to acquire it for use as a community policing center. The lessee challenged the decision to condemn the property for this purpose. In support of the petition, the lessee pointed out that the subject parcel was one of only 14 properties available in the City of Schenectady to operate its establishment since the passage of a 1999 zoning ordinance limiting the area in which adult bookstores could be operated. The appellate court found that it was not inclined to accede to the lessee's request to inquire into the county's motives for condemning the property. Instead, the appellate court found that the lessee made no argument that the underlying proceeding was unconstitutional, that the acquisition was made without the requisite authority, or that it was not for a legitimate public purpose. Moreover, the appellate court found no basis for sustaining any of its perceived procedural infirmities.


OUTCOME: The determination was affirmed.

 

694 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Morgan v. Grzesik


(1104) CAF 01-00475.


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


287 A.D.2d 150; 732 N.Y.S.2d 773; 2001 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10780



November 9, 2001, Decided  
November 9, 2001, Filed



DISPOSITION: Trial court order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: An order of the Oneida County Family Court (New York) granted petitioner visitation with her grandchildren pursuant to N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 72, despite the opposition of respondent parents, who appealed.


OVERVIEW: Respondents contended that, in light of Troxel, § 72 was unconstitutional on its face. The appeals court concluded that the specific manner in which the statute was applied by the trial court did not violate the parents' fundamental right under the Due Process Clause to make decisions concerning the care, custody, and control of their children. The instant case differed significantly from Troxel. Unlike the trial court in Troxel, the instant trial court did not presume that the grandmother's request for visitation would be granted in the absence of a showing that the children would be adversely impacted. Instead, by requiring the grandmother to establish standing, the court gave the parents' decision some presumptive or "special" weight, which is all Troxel required. The instant court further concluded that the record supported the trial court's determination that visitation was in the best interests of the children.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Parent and Child - Visitation Rights of Grandparents - Constitutionality of Domestic Relations Law § 72 

1. Domestic Relations Law § 72, which authorizes a grandparent to petition for visitation rights where "either or both ... parents ... is or are deceased, or where circumstances show that conditions exist which equity would see fit to intervene," is not unconstitutional on its face under the holding of Troxel v Granville (530 US 57) in which a state statute authorizing "any person" to "petition the court for visitation rights at any time" was declared unconstitutional as infringing upon the fundamental due process rights of parents to rear their children. In contrast to the statute declared unconstitutional in Troxel, Domestic Relations Law § 72 is narrowly limited to grandparents and does not grant automatic standing. Rather, in deciding that threshold question, the court is required to examine all the relevant facts, including the nature and basis of the parents' objection to visitation, and the nature and extent of the grandparent-grandchild relationship.

Parent and Child - Visitation Rights of Grandparents - Due Process Requirements

2. Family Court's application of Domestic Relations Law § 72 to grant petitioner maternal grandmother visitation rights to her five minor grandchildren over the objection of respondent parents is not violative of respondents' due process rights under the holding of Troxel v Granville (530 US 57) in which a state statute authorizing "any person" to "petition the court for visitation rights at any time" was declared unconstitutional as infringing upon the fundamental due process rights of parents to rear their children. The specific manner in which Domestic Relations Law § 72 was applied here does not violate the fundamental right of respondents to make decisions concerning the care, custody, and control of their children. Family Court, in determining that petitioner had standing to petition for visitation, considered the fact that respondents' objection was primarily based upon a child abuse report by petitioner which resulted in an "indicated" finding of abuse. The court further considered the children's close and loving relationship with petitioner, and considered the best interests of the children only after determining that petitioner had standing to contest respondents' decision. The court did not presume that petitioner's request for visitation would be granted in the absence of a showing that the children would be adversely impacted. Instead, by requiring petitioner to establish standing, the court gave respondents' decision the "special" weight required by due process. Lastly, the court, after fully considering the nature and basis of respondents' objections, crafted a visitation order addressing those concerns, directing the parties not to make disparaging remarks about each other in the children's presence.

 

695 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Soule v. Scalci


89428


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


288 A.D.2d 585; 732 N.Y.S.2d 662; 2001 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10339



November 8, 2001, Decided  
November 8, 2001, Entered



DISPOSITION: Trial court judgment reversed in part and affirmed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Supreme Court, Otsego County, New York, granted respondent owner's application, in a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, to review a determination of a zoning board of appeals (ZBA) that the definition of a "tourist accommodation" (TA) in the zoning ordinance of the Village of Cooperstown, New York, included the owner's five-unit apartment house. Appellants, a zoning enforcement officer and others, appealed.


OVERVIEW: A TA included a building intended, designed, and used as a private residence where overnight accommodations were rented from time to time to guests. Because the apartment house was occupied on a temporary basis by persons with out-of-state vehicle licenses, the owner was found in violation of the ordinance, as he had no special permit to operate a TA. The trial court declared the TA provisions invalid, as a word was missing from the definition. Appellants argued that the trial court erred in concluding that the definition was facially defective. The appellate division found that the trial court correctly concluded that the owner was improperly found in violation of the TA provision, as there was no evidence that he rented rooms in his private residence and the ZBA's conclusion that the transient rentals were sufficient to render the apartment house a tourist accommodation lacked a rational basis given the absence of such a restriction in the ordinance. Because the ZBA's ruling that the TA provisions applied to the owner was irrational, there was no need for the trial court to declare the provisions facially invalid and the judgment had to be modified to that extent.


OUTCOME: The appellate division modified the judgment, without costs, by reversing so much thereof as declared the tourist accommodation provisions of the zoning ordinance invalid and enjoined enforcement thereof, and affirmed the judgment as so modified.

 

696 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Gee


(994), KA 98-05472


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


286 A.D.2d 62; 730 N.Y.S.2d 810; 2001 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9121



September 28, 2001, Decided  
September 28, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected October 12, 2001.
Post-conviction relief denied at People v. Gee, 294 A.D.2d 965, 742 N.Y.S.2d 179, 2002 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6963 (N.Y. App. Div. 4th Dep't, 2002)
Appeal granted by People v. Gee, 98 N.Y.2d 651, 772 N.E.2d 612, 2002 N.Y. LEXIS 2067, 745 N.Y.S.2d 509 (2002)
Motion granted by People v. Gee, 2002 N.Y. LEXIS 1484 (N.Y., June 4, 2002)
Affirmed by People v. Gee, 99 N.Y.2d 158, 782 N.E.2d 1155, 2002 N.Y. LEXIS 3788, 753 N.Y.S.2d 19 (2002)
Post-conviction relief denied at People v. Gee, 2015 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2404, 2015 NY Slip Op 2385 (N.Y. App. Div. 4th Dep't, Mar. 20, 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his robbery conviction in Monroe County Court (New York), arguing counsel ineffectiveness, prosecutorial misconduct, and failure to provide him with notice, pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 710.30, of intent to provide eyewitness identification evidence based on out-of-court videotape viewing.


OVERVIEW: A convenience store clerk viewed a surveillance videotape, and still photographs derived from it, shortly after a robbery. Defendant was not identified and arrested until some time afterward. An appeals court reviewing his robbery conviction held that the only issue preserved for review was whether the prosecution was entitled to rely on the clerk's in-court identification of defendant, since it had not notified the defense pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 710.30 of the earlier viewings by the clerk. The appeals court indicated that defendant mistook the purpose of the statutory provision, which was to prevent unduly suggestive identification procedures orchestrated by police. When the clerk viewed the videotape and the stills, there was no proposed identification at all; she was simply helping police determine which photographs depicted the robbers. Under the circumstances, the identification by the victim was not based on the viewing, and no notice was required.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of conviction.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Identification of Defendant - Store Clerk's Viewing of Surveillance Video of Robbery and Composite Stills

1. Defendant, convicted of robbery in the first degree arising out of his forcible theft of cash from a convenience store clerk, is not entitled to suppression or preclusion of identification evidence in connection with the clerk's viewings of the store surveillance video and photographs depicting defendant's commission of the robbery. The store clerk initially viewed the store security videotape at the police station within a short time after the robbery and pointed out the robbers. The clerk's subsequent viewing of composite stills from the surveillance video showing the crime as it occurred was not unduly suggestive, and thus did not impermissibly taint the subsequent lineup and in-court identification. The clerk's viewing of the surveillance photos, without any overt or tacit attempts by police to link the photographs to a particular suspect or known individual, did not constitute a police-arranged identification procedure. At the time of the viewing, defendant was not an identified suspect, but a perpetrator of unknown identity. The police showed the clerk the stills in order to ascertain for themselves which of the individuals entering the store had committed the robbery. Consequently, the viewing was not an identification procedure, but rather an investigatory measure designed to allow the clerk to report the crime. Moreover, even assuming that the procedure utilized by the police was an identification procedure, it was not "unnecessarily suggestive." There is no constitutional infirmity in showing a crime victim or other eyewitness photographs of the crime itself. No due process concerns of fairness are raised by a procedure that enhances the eyewitness's memory of the crime and hence the accuracy and reliability of the eyewitness's identification. In any event, the viewing in this case created absolutely no risk of misidentification by the clerk, because the surveillance photographs depict the perpetrators in the act.

Crimes - Disclosure - Notice of Intention to Offer Evidence - Store Clerk's Viewing of Surveillance Video of Robbery

2. In defendant's robbery prosecution, arising out of his forcible theft of cash from a convenience store clerk, the People were not required to give defendant notice of their intention to offer identification evidence pursuant to CPL 710.30 regarding the clerk's viewings of the store surveillance video on the night of the robbery. Such a viewing did not constitute a police-arranged identification procedure that required the statutory notice. The police had no suspects and did not ask the clerk to identify any particular suspect or known individual as the robber. Instead, the police merely asked the clerk who among the persons in the store that night had committed the robbery. They further sought to confirm that the surveillance video was an accurate depiction of the robbery and robbers. The clerk then identified the perpetrators, but did not identify defendant "as such." Neither the clerk's viewing of the surveillance video nor anything said to or by the clerk during that viewing was at all probative of defendant's guilt or innocence. Moreover, to point out an unknown criminal actor on videotape is akin to giving a description of the appearance of that individual at the time of the crime. Such observations or descriptions are not within the ambit of CPL 710.30. Under the circumstances, the clerk's viewing of the surveillance video had no potential for suggestiveness, and it is thus not the kind of police-orchestrated identification procedure of which a defendant is entitled to notice.

 

697 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Westchester Creek Corp. v. N.Y. City Sch. Constr. Auth.


4650 M-2505


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


286 A.D.2d 154; 730 N.Y.S.2d 95; 2001 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8478



September 13, 2001, Decided  
September 13, 2001, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner development corporation (developer) sought review of a determination of respondent city school construction authority to condemn property which had been leased to the developer by the city. The petition was brought pursuant to the N.Y. Em. Dom. Proc. Law § 207.


OVERVIEW: The developer argued the school construction authority exceeded its statutory authority, violated its due process rights, and violated the Contract Clause of U.S. Const. art. I, § 10, when it decided to condemn the developer's leasehold property for use as a public school. The developer argued its leasehold interest was a prior public interest that should be protected. The court found that although lands already acquired for public use should not be taken for another public use unless there were special, unusual, and peculiar reasons, construction of a public school was such a reason, within the contemplation of the New York City School Construction Act, N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law § 1725 et seq. The developer contended it had already partially installed a drainage system and had vested rights in the property. The court found the developer would be justly compensated for the condemnation of its interest, and that powers essential to the operation of government could not be contracted away and were not subject to the Contract Clause. The school construction authority was a public benefit corporation and construction of a school was an essential governmental operation.


OUTCOME: The petition was dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Eminent Domain - Public Use - Condemnation of Lessee's Interest in Urban Renewal Property for Public School Construction - Condemnation Powers of New York City School Construction Authority

1. Property acquired by the City of New York for public use in connection with a commercial urban development project and then leased to petitioner as a redeveloper under an approved urban renewal plan can thereafter be lawfully condemned by respondent New York City School Construction Authority for construction of a public school. The proposed acquisition does not exceed respondent's broad authority to acquire property by condemnation for school construction ( Public Authorities Law § 1728 [6]). While urban development is considered a "superior use" to retention of property in the hands of a private owner, school construction is no less "superior" a public use than urban redevelopment.

Eminent Domain - Public Use - Prior Public Use Doctrine

2. Property acquired by the City of New York for public use in connection with a commercial urban development project and then leased to petitioner as a redeveloper under an approved urban renewal plan can thereafter be lawfully condemned by respondent New York City School Construction Authority for construction of a public school. Acquisition of land for construction of a public school is a use that is special, unusual and peculiar within the contemplation of the New York City School Construction Authority Act, and therefore the prior public use doctrine does not bar condemnation by respondent Authority.

Constitutional Law - Taking of Property - Condemnation of Lessee's Interest in Urban Renewal Property for Public School Construction

3. Property acquired by the City of New York for public use in connection with a commercial urban development project and then leased to petitioner as a redeveloper under an approved urban renewal plan can thereafter be lawfully condemned by respondent New York City School Construction Authority for construction of a public school. Petitioner's status as a lessee does not afford it any constitutional protection against the condemnation of its interest in the subject property. To the extent article XVIII, § 1 of the New York State Constitution encompasses a commercial project, it does not go so far as to confer any protected status on the redeveloper. The provision for incidental or appurtenant facilities is sufficiently broad to include a neighborhood school within the scope of an urban renewal project.

Constitutional Law - Taking of Property - Condemnation of Lessee's Interest in Urban Renewal Property for Public School Construction

4. Property acquired by the City of New York for public use in connection with a commercial urban development project and then leased to petitioner as a redeveloper under an approved urban renewal plan can thereafter be lawfully condemned by respondent New York City School Construction Authority for construction of a public school. Condemnation by respondent authority will not result in an unconstitutional impairment of petitioner's lease with the City in violation of the Contract Clause (US Const, art I, § 10). The condemnor is a public benefit corporation created by the Legislature and is an independent agency of the State, not the City. Moreover, respondent has conceded that the exercise of the power of eminent domain is subject to the obligation to pay just compensation to the condemnee, and the value of a lease may be determined by economic factors.

Frontier Ins. Co. v. Town Bd.


89210


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


285 A.D.2d 953; 728 N.Y.S.2d 311; 2001 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7631



July 26, 2001, Decided  
July 26, 2001, Entered



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant insurance company challenged the judgment of the Supreme Court, Sullivan County (New York) which, in an action for a declaratory judgment, granted respondents sewer district, property owner association, and the town board's cross motions for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: An insurance company sought to invalidate by means of a declaratory judgment a local law assessing fees for sewage disposal, contending that the law violated state and local laws, as well as the state and federal constitutions. The trial court granted the motions of the town board, the sewer district, and the property owners' association for summary judgment and dismissed the claim. The insurance company appealed. The appellate court found that because sewer usage was incapable of calculation with exactitude, discrepancy in charges between various properties within the sewer district could be tolerated so long as a rational basis existed with respect to the rents assessed. Further, the appellate court found that the company also failed to show such flagrant and palpable inequality between the burden imposed and the benefit received as to amount to the arbitrary taking of property without compensation.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

Koultukis v. Phillips


4004


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


285 A.D.2d 433; 728 N.Y.S.2d 440; 2001 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7657



July 26, 2001, Decided  
July 26, 2001, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant agency appealed the order of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York) temporarily enjoining it from proceeding with an impending padlock enforcement action.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff tenant operated a chiropractic office on defendant landlord's premises. The premises was located in a residential district. Medical offices were allowable in residential areas, which under City of New York, N.Y., Zoning Resolution § 22-14 encompassed medical offices or group medical centers, including the practice of dentistry or osteopathy. The agency commenced a padlock enforcement action against the tenant for violating § 22-14, because the agency did not consider a chiropractic office to be a medical office under § 22-14. Defendants building manager and successor in interest sought to enjoin the padlock enforcement action. The grant of injunctive relief was an abuse of discretion. Because this action did not involve a constitutional issue, the claim was to be reviewed at the administrative level before judicial review could occur and not all administrative remedies had been exhausted. The preliminary injunction was improper, because the agency's interpretation of § 22-14 was reasonable and no irreparable harm was shown.


OUTCOME: The order was reversed, and the temporary injunction was vacated.

 

700 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Patrolmen's Benevolent Ass'n v. City of New York


89417


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


285 A.D.2d 52; 729 N.Y.S.2d 789; 2001 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7388; 169 L.R.R.M. 3004



July 12, 2001, Decided  
July 12, 2001, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Counsel Amended October 30, 2001.



DISPOSITION: Order and judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city appealed the order of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York) granting plaintiff labor union's motion for a declaration stating that the State Public Employment Relations Board (State board) had exclusive jurisdiction over the city's scope of bargaining petition and denying the city's cross motion seeking a declaration that N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 212(3) was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The union, negotiating on behalf of city police, and the city were negotiating a new collective bargaining agreement. The city filed a scope of bargaining petition with defendant local public employment relations board (local board). The union responded by asserting that the state board had sole jurisdiction over the scope of bargaining petition under N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 212(3). The city claimed that § 212(3) was unconstitutional under New York's home rule provision. N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 2(b)(2). N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 212(3) was constitutional, because the statute was a general law, and it uniformly prohibited localities from prohibiting police unions from utilizing the state board. The state agency had exclusive jurisdiction over scope of bargaining disputes between the union and the city.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law - Validity of Statute - General and Special Laws - Subjecting Municipal Police and Firefighters to PERB Impasse Procedures

1. Chapter 641 of the Laws of 1998, which amended the Taylor Law (Civil Service Law § 212 [3]) so as to vest the Public Employment Relations Board (PERB) with exclusive jurisdiction to resolve scope of bargaining disputes arising in the course of collective negotiations between a municipality and its police and fire departments, is not unconstitutional as violative of the home rule provision of the State Constitution (NY Const, art IX, § 2 [b] [2]). The statute uniformly prohibits every local government from preventing its police and fire unions from utilizing PERB. The fact that the additional provision permitting a public employee union to opt to continue dispute resolutions procedures before an existing local body applies to only four localities in the State does not render the statute a special law. The procedures of the local bodies are the substantial equivalent of the corresponding procedures of PERB, and the option of determining which administrative body has exclusive jurisdiction rests with the public employee organization. Moreover, even if chapter 641 is a special law, it falls within the exception to the home rule procedural requirements, since it serves a substantial State concern. The State clearly has a substantial interest in the speedy resolution of labor disputes between its municipalities and their police and fire departments.

Constitutional Law - Validity of Statute - Subjecting Municipal Police and Firefighters to PERB Impasse Procedures - No Improper Delegation of Legislative Power

2. Chapter 641 of the Laws of 1998, which amended the Taylor Law (Civil Service Law § 212 [3]) so as to vest the Public Employment Relations Board (PERB) with exclusive jurisdiction to resolve scope of bargaining disputes arising in the course of collective negotiations between a municipality and its police and fire departments, does not unconstitutionally delegate legislative power to the police and fire unions to the extent that it allows those organizations to opt to continue dispute resolutions procedures before an existing local body. Permitting a public employee union to opt to use PERB does not amend or repeal the legislation which created the local bodies. The statute simply prevents a municipality with its own board from requiring its police or firefighter unions to use that board, rather than choosing to use PERB. It is not a delegation of any legislative authority, nor does it delegate to the public employee organizations the authority to resolve bargaining impasses or any other governmental function.

Civil Service - Public Employees' Fair Employment Act - Exclusive Jurisdiction of PERB to Resolve Scope of Bargaining Disputes between City of New York and Police Union

3. Chapter 641 of the Laws of 1998, which amended the Taylor Law (Civil Service Law § 212 [3]) so as to vest the Public Employment Relations Board (PERB) with exclusive jurisdiction to resolve scope of bargaining disputes arising in the course of collective negotiations between a municipality and its police and fire departments, is constitutional. Consequently, pursuant to the statute, PERB has exclusive jurisdiction over any impasse which may arise during collective negotiations between the City of New York and its police force. PERB's exclusive jurisdiction over impasse and negotiation issues (Civil Service Law § 209) must necessarily include scope of bargaining issues. Furthermore, since scope of bargaining issues do not generally arise in improper practice proceedings within the meaning of Civil Service Law § 209-a, the jurisdiction of the City's Board of Collective Bargaining, where the available option has been exercised in favor of PERB, is limited to jurisdiction over improper practice disputes under Civil Service Law § 205 (5) (d). Accepting the City's contention that PERB's impasse authority does not include scope of bargaining issues would create the "absurdity" of having two separate agencies on two different levels of government attempting to separately resolve the intertwined issues of scope of bargaining and impasse resolution.
