730 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Silver v. Pataki


1801


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


274 A.D.2d 57; 711 N.Y.S.2d 402; 2000 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8044



July 20, 2000, Decided  
July 20, 2000, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant governor appealed from an order of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), denying his motion to dismiss litigation brought against him by plaintiff speaker of the state senate and arising out of dispute over the state budget.


OVERVIEW: This case was a budgetary struggle between plaintiff, speaker of state senate and defendant governor. Despite no showing of authority for the institution of the litigation, the plaintiff was found by the lower court to have the inherent capacity as a legislator to obtain judicial review of his challenge to the defendant's exercise of veto power. Because recognition of such inherent capacity would confer an authority to sue contrary to constitutionally-distributed powers, and because the creation of the plaintiff's cause of action would needlessly propel the judiciary into future political conflicts, the court reversed. The threshold questions raised by the litigation were whether the party seeking judicial intervention had capacity and, if so, whether such party had standing. The plaintiff had not been authorized by the legislature to commence the action. The plaintiff was not a party based upon any purported legal injury to him. Even were the capacity issue deemed resolved on basis of the plaintiff's inherent authority, standing could not be found for the legislator lawsuit. The clerk was directed to enter judgment in favor of defendant dismissing the complaint.


OUTCOME: Judgment was reversed and clerk was directed to enter judgment in favor of defendant dismissing the complaint. The plaintiff had not been authorized by the legislature to commence the action, nor did he have standing to bring suit.


HEADNOTES

Legislature - Budget - Legal Capacity and Standing of Speaker of Assembly to Challenge Governor's Line-Item Vetoes

Plaintiff lacks the legal capacity as Member and Speaker of the New York State Assembly to challenge the Governor's line-item vetoes in nonappropriation "programmatic" budget bills as unconstitutional under NY Constitution, article IV, § 7. While budget litigation involving the nature and scope of the delegated authority shared by the Executive and the Legislature may present questions which are "justiciable," a "justiciable" controversy can only be presented by a party with the capacity and standing to litigate. The Speaker of the Assembly is a legislative officer who appoints committee chairs, allocates staff, expedites or impedes action on bills, and administers the rules internal to the Assembly. The Speaker has no express delegation of power to obtain judicial review against the Governor. There is no express legal authorization enabling the Speaker to sue, nor has the Legislature conferred capacity on the Speaker. The Speaker has not been prevented from discharging his legal duties as either Speaker or as Member by any action of the Governor. Moreover, even if the Speaker has the inherent authority to litigate this action, the Speaker lacks standing. The Speaker has made no allegation of any personal harm nor any remediable interference with the performance of his duties. The institutional injury alleged does not constitute sufficient injury to confer standing.

People v. Edwards


11316


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


274 A.D.2d 754; 712 N.Y.S.2d 71; 2000 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8060

Defendant later pleaded guilty to first-degree murder under a plea agreement approved by county court, on condition that the death notice was withdrawn. It affirmed the suppression ruling, concluding the State proved the voluntariness of defendant's statements.

Schulz v. State


85171


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


274 A.D.2d 615; 710 N.Y.S.2d 702; 2000 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7605



July 6, 2000, Decided  
July 6, 2000, Entered



DISPOSITION: Judgment modified, on the law, without costs, by reversing so much thereof as directed that a new environmental impact statement be prepared for the entire Warren County Sewer Project and permanently enjoined respondent Warren County Board of Supervisors from undertaking any action in furtherance of the final design and construction of any portion or segment of such project pending adoption of said new environmental impact statement; a supplemental final environmental impact statement is to be prepared solely for the Queensbury service area and, pending acceptance of such supplemental final environmental impact statement, respondent Warren County Board of Supervisors is enjoined from further project activity only insofar as it relates to the Queensbury service area; and, as so modified,  [***2] affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent state and county officials appealed a Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), judgment granting petitioner landowners' motion to enjoin respondents from proceeding with a sewer project until completion of a completely new environmental review under the New York State Environmental Quality Review Act, N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law art. 8.

After completion of environmental quality review pursuant to the New York State Environmental Quality Review Act, N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law art. 8, petitioners obtained an order enjoining respondent state and county officials from continuing with the project in one of the three areas on grounds that review had been inadequate. The court held that the plain language of that order made it clear that the injunction related only to one specific area and refused to enjoin the whole project, since supplemental review of later additions or modifications could not be used as a pretext for correcting problems that petitioners perceived in the earlier review process.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment, to enjoin activity only as to that area for which prior environmental review had been ruled inadequate, because the original order clearly stated that review was adequate as to the remaining area.

 

733 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Doe


11699


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


271 A.D.2d 29; 710 N.Y.S.2d 420; 2000 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7430



June 29, 2000, Decided  
June 29, 2000, Entered



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed from an order of the County Court of Albany County (New York), which granted defendants' motion to dismiss a sealed indictment after presentment of the case to the grand jury but prior to defendants' arraignment.


OVERVIEW: Defendants obtained preliminary injunctive relief prohibiting the state Attorney General from criminally prosecuting them for an alleged violation of a Medicaid regulation pending resolution of their declaratory judgment action seeking to strike the regulation as unconstitutionally vague. The Attorney General submitted charges to a grand jury and obtained an indictment. When the State attempted to have defendants arraigned on this indictment, the court refused to arraign them unless and until the preliminary injunction was lifted. Defendants then sought dismissal of the indictment by the court, which was granted. The State appealed, claiming that the court was without jurisdiction to grant defendants' motion to dismiss. The court affirmed, rejecting the State's claim of lack of personal jurisdiction. The court had subject matter jurisdiction over the indictment, the filing of which marked the commencement of the criminal action in that court. The subject indictment was obtained in direct violation of a binding court order and was therefore illegally obtained. Thus, it was well within the inherent power of the lower court to dismiss the same.


OUTCOME: The order that granted the motion and dismissed defendants' indictment was affirmed because the indictment was obtained in direct violation of a court order and was within the inherent power of the county court to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Indictment - Power of County Court to Dismiss Illegally Obtained Indictment Prior to Defendants' Arraignment

Where the Attorney General obtained a sealed indictment charging defendants with violating a Medicaid regulation in direct violation of a preliminary injunction issued by Supreme Court prohibiting the Attorney General from criminally prosecuting defendants pending resolution of their declaratory judgment action seeking to strike the regulation as unconstitutionally vague, County Court had the inherent power to dismiss the illegally obtained indictment prior to defendants' arraignment. County Court clearly had subject matter jurisdiction over the indictment and did not exceed its power in dismissing it. Moreover, although CPL 210.20 seemingly requires a defendant's arraignment prior to a motion to dismiss, the statute does not restrict the time period within which a court may dismiss an indictment. Requiring defendants to appear in open court for formal arraignment before obtaining the judicial relief to which they are entitled would merely elevate form over substance, and could result in harm to defendants since the mere unsealing of the indictment could result in their immediate disqualification from continued participation in the Medicaid program (see, 18 NYCRR 515.7).

 

734 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Energy Ass'n v. PSC


78608


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


273 A.D.2d 708; 710 N.Y.S.2d 662; 2000 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7453



June 29, 2000, Decided  
June 29, 2000, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected November 2, 2000.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners utility customer and public interest group appealed a judgment of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), dismissing their petition seeking review of a determination of respondent Public Service Commission setting forth a plan for competition in the electric utility industry.


OVERVIEW: Various petitioners challenged respondent New York utilities agency's adoption of a plan for moving toward competition in the electrical industry; petitioner taxpayer and public interest group sought review after their petition was dismissed. The court reviewed for standing, and held that it was absent. The statute giving taxpayers standing to seek review of government actions applied only to determinations relating directly to fiscal matters. In this case, the nexus to fiscal matters was simply too indefinite to permit standing. A person aggrieved by an administrative determination had common law standing to seek review, but that required demonstrating an injury in fact, falling within the zone of interest the relevant statute sought to protect and different in kind or degree from that suffered by the general public; petitioners could not show such aggrieved status. Finally, petitioners could not show organizational standing because no one member could show standing to sue.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed dismissal of the petition, because petitioners lacked taxpayer standing, because they could not show sufficient nexus to state fiscal activities, common law standing, because petitioners were not parties aggrieved by the administrative determination, or organizational standing, because no member could show standing.

Santiago v. Bristol


(845.1) OP 00-01131


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


273 A.D.2d 813; 709 N.Y.S.2d 724; 2000 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5962; 31 Media L. Rep. 2215



June 16, 2000, Decided  
May 25, 2000, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner accused commenced an action under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78, which sought a judgment prohibiting respondent judge from enforcing an order that declared N.Y. Civ. Rights Law § 52 unconstitutional pursuant to N.Y. Const. art. I, § 8 and thereby permitted audiovisual broadcast of petitioner's criminal trial by intervenor news media.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner accused filed an action under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 and sought a writ of prohibition to prevent respondent judge from permitting intervenor news media to broadcast petitioner's criminal trial. Respondent had declared N.Y. Civ. Rights Law § 52 unconstitutional pursuant to N.Y. Const. art. I, § 8, which prohibited the televising, broadcasting, or taking motion pictures of a trial. OUTCOME: The court granted the petition and judgment in favor of petitioner accused and prohibited respondent judge from permitting audiovisual broadcast of petitioner's criminal trial by intervenor news media. The court found that intervenors had no constitutional or statutory right to broadcast petitioner's criminal trial and that respondent was without authority to allow the broadcast.

Raquet v. J.M. Braun Builders, Inc.


(921), CA 00-00053


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


273 A.D.2d 850; 709 N.Y.S.2d 292; 2000 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6845



June 16, 2000, Decided  
June 16, 2000, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff claimants brought an action against defendants, construction companies and their owners. A prior order dismissed the action. Subsequently, N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 205-a was enacted, which expanded the rights of injured firefighters, and the action was revived. The Supreme Court of Erie County (New York) denied new motions to dismiss filed by the companies and their owners. The companies and their owners appealed.


OVERVIEW: The construction companies and their owners contended that they were entitled to rely on the court's prior affirmance of the dismissal of the action and that the enactment of § 205-a deprived them of property without due process of law. The court disagreed. The legislature could constitutionally revive a personal cause of action if the circumstances were exceptional and were such as to satisfy the court that serious injustice would result to plaintiffs not guilty of any fault if the intention of the legislature were not effectuated. Section 205-a, which ameliorated the harsh effects of the common-law firefighters rule, met that standard. The trial court properly denied dismissal.


OUTCOME: The order denying the motions to dismiss of the construction companies and their owners was affirmed.

People v. Maldonado


10145


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


273 A.D.2d 537; 711 N.Y.S.2d 191; 2000 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6694



June 15, 2000, Decided  
June 15, 2000, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected November 2, 2000.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is modified, on the law and the facts, by reversing so much thereof as convicted defendant of the crime of aggravated harassment of an employee by an inmate; the first count of indictment No. 96-537 is dismissed; and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed jury convictions in County Court of Broome County (New York), for criminal sale of controlled substance in third degree, aggravated harassment of employee by inmate, resisting arrest, criminal mischief in fourth degree, and criminal possession of controlled substance in seventh degree.


OVERVIEW: Upon arrest for disorderly conduct, defendant resisted handcuffing, kicked at officers, and banged his head against the police van, damaging the vehicle. After being placed in a secure "lockup," he continuously, according to videotape and testimony, spit blood and spittle around the area and at personnel. He was ultimately convicted of aggravated harassment of an employee by an inmate under N.Y. Penal Law §240.32, resisting arrest, criminal mischief for damages to the van and lockup door, and criminal possession and sale of a controlled substance. He appealed, claiming insufficient evidence to sustain the drug convictions, error in county court's failure to order a mental competency examination, and that he was not an "inmate" under the statute. The court modified the judgment, finding the statute's restrictive language did eliminate defendant as an "inmate" and required reversal on aggravated harassment, but affirmed as modified, finding no error in remaining convictions.


OUTCOME: Judgment was modified by reversing portion convicting defendant of aggravated harassment of employee by inmate, as statute's restrictive language regarding "inmate" excluded defendant, and affirmed as modified. Remaining contentions were without merit and there was no error in failing to question his mental competency despite his repugnant behavior.

People v. Barrows


1998-06328


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


273 A.D.2d 246; 710 N.Y.S.2d 251; 2000 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6304


September 17, 1999, Argued  
June 5, 2000, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Revised September 27, 2000.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant People challenged the order of the Kings County Supreme Court (New York) which granted respondent defendant's motion to set aside the jury verdict convicting respondent of attempted disseminating indecent material to minors in the first degree, N.Y. Penal Law §235.22.


OVERVIEW: Respondent defendant was convicted of attempted disseminating indecent material to minors in the first degree, N.Y. Penal Law §235.22. The trial court granted respondent's motion to set aside the jury verdict, holding that the statute was unconstitutionally vague, overbroad, and, to the extent the statute barred interstate communications, it also violated the Commerce Clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, cl. 3. The court reversed the order, concluding that the statute did not violate to Commerce Clause. The court noted that the statute represented a legitimate exercise of the state's police power and did not discriminate against or burden interstate trade. Further, the court determined the statute was not unconstitutionally overbroad. The court noted that the statute should be read as requiring that an individual intend to initiate the dissemination of indecent material to a minor and thereby further intend to importune, invite or induce the minor to engage in sexual conduct for the sender's benefit.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order and reinstated the jury verdict convicting respondent defendant of the crimes of attempted disseminating indecent material to minors in the first degree because the statute withstood constitutional scrutiny. The court remitted the matter for sentencing.

Tennessee Gas Pipeline Co. v. Urbach


86152


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


269 A.D.2d 19; 708 N.Y.S.2d 193; 2000 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5935; 145 Oil & Gas Rep. 233



May 25, 2000, Decided  
May 25, 2000, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended June 8, 2000.



DISPOSITION: The order is modified, on the law, with costs to defendants, by reversing so much thereof as dismissed the complaint; it is declared that Tax Law §§ 189, 189-a and 189-b have not been shown to be facially unconstitutional; and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed the order of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York) granting defendant's motion for summary judgment and dismissing plaintiff's action for a declaration that the Natural Gas Import Tax, N.Y. Tax Law §§ 189, 189-a, 189-b, was unconstitutional under U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, cl. 3.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff corporation was an out-of-state transporter of natural gas. Defendant tax commissioner sought to impose the Natural Gas Import Tax, N.Y. Tax Law §§ 189, 189-a, 189-b on plaintiff's consumption of natural gas in the state. Plaintiff sought a declaration that the tax was unconstitutional because it violated the Commerce Clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, cl. 3. Plaintiff appealed the grant defendant's motion for summary judgment. The court reversed in part insofar as plaintiff's complaint was dismissed and affirmed the order as modified. Plaintiff's constitutional challenge was premature, and plaintiff was required to exhaust administrative remedies. Defendant was entitled to a judgment that the tax was not shown to be facially unconstitutional. Plaintiff failed to show that there was no set of circumstances under which the tax was valid. Nothing in the tax revealed that it discriminated against interstate commerce. The constitutional claim required resolution of factual issues at the administrative level to develop the record.


OUTCOME: The order was modified in part insofar as the order dismissed the complaint and was affirmed as modified. Plaintiff was required to pursue any available administrative remedies before making a constitutional challenge, and defendant was entitled to a judgment declaring that the tax was not shown to be facially unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Taxation - Natural Gas Import Tax - Constitutionality under Commerce Clause

The Natural Gas Import Tax (NGIT), which imposes on "every gas importer a monthly privilege tax ... on the privilege or act of importing gas services or causing gas services to be imported into this state for its own use or consumption in this state" (Tax Law § 189 [2] [a]), is not facially unconstitutional under the Commerce Clause. The statute does not impermissibly discriminate against out-of-State suppliers who import natural gas into New York for consumption without imposing a similar tax upon New York suppliers who sell natural gas within New York. Rather, the NGIT was enacted to equalize the tax treatment of out-of-State and in-State suppliers of natural gas. Prior to the passage of the NGIT, consumers of gas services could avoid the burden of taxes imposed by Tax Law § 186 and 186-a by purchasing the service out of State and hiring transportation to carry that service to the consumer's premises in this State. The NGIT was enacted to impose taxes and surcharges upon out-of-State gas importers commensurate to those imposed upon in-State suppliers under sections 186 and 186-a. Moreover, the fact that some in-State providers of natural gas not regulated by the Public Service Commission are not required to pass through the tax imposed under sections 186 and 186-a to consumers does not establish that the NGIT is facially defective. The relevant inquiry is whether there is no set of circumstances under which the statute would be valid, and not whether the statute might operate unconstitutionally under some conceivable set of circumstances.

Markham v. Comstock


(777), CA 99-1171


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


272 A.D.2d 971; 708 N.Y.S.2d 674; 2000 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5138



May 10, 2000, Decided  
May 10, 2000, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners and respondents appealed from orders granting and denying portions of motions for summary judgment from Supreme Court, Yates County (New York) in action challenging increase in assessed value of petitioners' summer home and constitutionality of N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 425.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner nonresident property owners challenged the assessed value of their summer home. On appeal, the court held that summary judgment was properly denied on the assessment's excessiveness because plaintiffs raised triable issues whether the improvements increased the property's market value. Whether respondents acted in bad faith in making the assessment did not bear on the correctness of the assessment. The court did not lack jurisdiction over petitioner's constitutional challenge to N.Y. Real Prop. Tax § 425. The limitation of the school tax relief exemption to property serving as a primary residence did not favor residents over nonresidents because it applied to property that was not the owner's primary residence whether the owner was a New York resident or not. Because residents and nonresidents were treated alike, U.S. Const. art. IV, § 2 was not implicated. The limitation was also rationally related to a legitimate state interest.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed in part and denied in part because triable questions were raised on whether assessment was excessive, supreme court had jurisdiction over constitutional challenge, and limitation of tax relief exemption violated neither privileges and immunities clause or equal protection clause.

Rosenbaum v. City of New York


2149


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


272 A.D.2d 91; 707 N.Y.S.2d 410; 2000 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5427



May 9, 2000, Decided  
May 9, 2000, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff owner of property moved for partial summary judgment seeking discharge of liens held by defendant City of New York for repair work done on plaintiff's property and defendant cross-moved for summary judgment dismissing the complaint on the ground of untimeliness.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff purchased a piece of property on which defendant City of New York had performed repairs for the previous owner. Prior to purchase, defendant had not formally filed a lien. After plaintiff bought the property, defendant filed a lien for the repair work. Plaintiff sought a discharge of the alleged improperly placed liens and damages for slander of title. Plaintiff filed a motion for summary judgment that he was a good faith subsequent purchaser of the property and, therefore, not required to pay the lien. Defendant filed a cross-motion for summary judgment that plaintiff's action was time-barred because it should have been filed within four months as an "Article 78" proceeding. The court denied both motions. The court held a question remained as to whether plaintiff was a bona fide good faith purchaser because plaintiff's pre-purchase title search showed a lis pendens was filed against the premises and he had the same attorney as the administrator of the lis pendens. Since plaintiff's action was not required to be brought under Article 78 the four month time period was inapplicable.


OUTCOME: The court denied both motions for summary judgment, finding that a question of fact remained as to whether plaintiff was a subsequent purchaser in good faith and entitled to dismissal of the liens, thus summary judgment was precluded; plaintiff's action was for slander of title and was therefore subject to a one year and not a four month statute of limitations. Consequently, the action was timely.

Lang v. Pataki


567


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


271 A.D.2d 375; 707 N.Y.S.2d 90; 2000 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4584



April 27, 2000, Decided  
April 27, 2000, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs appealed the order of the New York County Supreme Court (New York), which, upon the parties' respective motions for summary judgment, declared N.Y. Real Prop. Acts. Law §§ 745(2), 747-a, to be constitutional on their face.


OVERVIEW: The trial court declared N.Y. Real Prop. Acts. Law §§ 745(2), 747-a, to be constitutional on their face. The court affirmed. The court determined there was no merit to plaintiffs' contention that § 747-a violated the separation of powers doctrine. The court noted that under the N.Y. Const. art. VI, § 30, the authority to regulate practice and procedure in the courts was delegated primarily to the legislature. Similarly without merit was plaintiffs' contention that N.Y. Real Prop. Acts. Law § 745(2) violated the separation of powers doctrine. The court noted that while it was possible that § 745(2) could be applied in particular cases in a manner that deprived a tenant of due process, the mere possibility did not serve to render the provision invalid on its face.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order because there was no merit to plaintiffs' contentions that the challenged statutes violated the separation of powers doctrine. The authority to regulate practice and procedure in the courts was delegated primarily to the legislature.

Willock v. Schenectady Mun. Hous. Auth.


83894


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT

April 20, 2000, Entered

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner appealed from the judgment of the Supreme Court, Schenectady County (New York), which dismissed petitioner's application, in a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78, to review a determination of respondent evicting petitioner from a Federally subsidized apartment for violation of the lease.


OVERVIEW: This case arose out of petitioner's eviction from Federally subsidized housing project, after marijuana was found in her apartment. The Hearing Officer concluded that petitioner had violated the lease by virtue of the presence of marijuana in her apartment and that she should be evicted. Petitioner's argument that the inspection of the apartment constituted an unconstitutional search and seizure was rejected because sufficient notice was given that petitioner's apartment could be inspected for the presence of controlled substances.


OUTCOME: Judgment was affirmed because it was clear that marijuana was found in petitioner's apartment, there was substantial evidence that petitioner violated an explicit material lease term, and petitioner's claims that she was unaware of the activity were unpersuasive because neither the lease nor the federal regulation required the element of knowledge.

Cimato Bros., Inc. v. Town of Pendleton


(379), CA 99-3443


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


270 A.D.2d 879; 705 N.Y.S.2d 468; 2000 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3654



March 29, 2000, Decided  
March 29, 2000, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant town filed an appeal from the judgment of the Supreme Court, Niagara County (New York) declaring that the inspection fee structure portion of its 1989 Public Improvement Permit Ordinance was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff contractor sued defendant town, alleging that the inspection fee portion of the defendant's 1989 Public Improvement Permit Ordinance was unconstitutional. The trial court found the statute to be unconstitutional and defendant appealed. Affirming, the court held local ordinances enjoy an exceedingly strong presumption of constitutionality. In challenging an ordinance, a party must rebut that presumption by establishing beyond a reasonable doubt that there was no reasonable basis for the challenged ordinance. Plaintiff sustained its burden by showing that the fee structure imposed upon contractors and developers for inspection services conducted by the defendant was not assessed or estimated on the basis of reliable factual studies or statistics. Further, plaintiff also established that requiring contractors and developers to pay the actual cost of inspection was not a commonly employed approach in the case of public improvement projects and the fee structure portion of the ordinance lacked uniformity and predictability.


OUTCOME: Statute unconstitutionality affirmed; plaintiff contractor showed the fee structure for inspection services conducted by the defendant town was not assessed or estimated on the basis of reliable factual studies or statistics, payment of actual costs of inspection was not a common approach, and the fee structure lacked uniformity and predictability.

 

749 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Barbieri v. Mount Sinai Hosp.


2198, 2199


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


264 A.D.2d 1; 706 N.Y.S.2d 8; 2000 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2833



March 14, 2000, Decided  
March 14, 2000, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York) granting partial summary judgment to plaintiff on liability and granting summary judgment to third-party defendant in a personal injury action.


OVERVIEW: Third-party defendant (a contractor) employed plaintiff as a carpenter. While working at defendant hospital to install insulation, plaintiff fell through an unmarked access panel underneath old insulation and debris in the ceiling. Plaintiff sued defendant, alleging severe and permanently disabling injuries. Defendant filed an indemnity and contribution claim against third-party defendant. Third-party defendant claimed, as an affirmative defense, that defendant's action was barred by N.Y. Work. Comp. Law § 11, which preserved workers' compensation immunity for employers in indemnity actions, except when plaintiffs suffered a "grave injury." The court held that plaintiff had not alleged sufficient facts to demonstrate he had suffered a grave injury. Plaintiff's cognitive injures were not severe enough to constitute permanent and disability: he was ambulatory, his long-term memory was intact, his cognitive ability was consistent with his academic history, and he only seemed to demonstrate some slowness of speech and distractibility.


OUTCOME: Court granted plaintiff's motion for partial summary judgment on liability and third-party defendant's motion for summary judgment on indemnity, dismissing third-party claim, because New York's Worker's Compensation law protected employers from such indemnity claims, and defendant could not make showing that plaintiff received grave injury triggering exception to this rule.


HEADNOTES

Workers' Compensation - Third-Party Action - Statutory Bar on Third-Party Action against Employer Unless Injured Worker Sustained "Grave Injury"

1. In an action pursuant to Labor Law § 240 (1), plaintiff's injuries, as pleaded and as amplified by the bill of particulars, do not constitute "grave" injuries sufficient to overcome the exclusivity bar of the Workers' Compensation Law and allow defendant premises owner to maintain a third-party action against plaintiff's employer sounding in contribution and indemnification based upon the negligent supervision and protection of plaintiff. Workers' Compensation Law § 11 bars actions in contribution or indemnification by third parties against the employer "unless such third person proves through competent medical evidence" that the injured worker sustained a "grave injury," defined as "one or more of the following: death, permanent and total loss of use or amputation of an arm, leg, hand or foot, loss of multiple fingers, loss of multiple toes, paraplegia or quadriplegia, total and permanent blindness, total and permanent deafness, loss of nose, loss of ear, permanent and severe facial disfigurement, loss of an index finger or an acquired injury to the brain caused by an external physical force resulting in permanent total disability." Here, plaintiff alleged only facial scarring, not "disfigurement." The scarring is never further described, and is, in any event, not described in any terms satisfying even a broad construction of "disfigurement." Furthermore, although the pleadings allege the permanency of plaintiff's cognitive injuries, the pleadings do not satisfy the statutory requirement for a "grave injury" that the loss be "total and permanent." While the medical evidence submitted indicates that plaintiff suffers periods of disorientation and some dementia, particularly in short-term memory, such medical evidence does not, on its face, make out permanent and total disability. Moreover, since discovery has been completed, defendant cannot claim that it lacked an opportunity to adduce adequate evidence in support of its contention that plaintiff suffered a "grave injury."

Workers' Compensation - Third-Party Action - Constitutionality of Statutory Bar on Third-Party Action against Employer Unless Injured Worker Sustained "Grave Injury"

2. In an action pursuant to Labor Law § 240 (1), defendant premises owner has failed to demonstrate the unconstitutionality, on due process and equal protection grounds, of Workers' Compensation Law § 11, which bars actions in contribution or indemnification by third parties against the employer of the injured worker "unless such third person proves through competent medical evidence" that the injured worker sustained a "grave injury." The statute is rationally related to the stated legislative objective of reducing liability insurance premiums and fostering a more favorable business climate. Moreover, even if there were other effective methods by which to accomplish the legislative objective, the courts should not substitute their judgment for that of the Legislature in determining the particular method to meet a given need.

 

750 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Reed


1997-10149, 1998-00747


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


265 A.D.2d 56; 705 N.Y.S.2d 592; 2000 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2802


September 21, 1999, Argued and Submitted  
March 13, 2000, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended April 13, 2000.



DISPOSITION: The judgments are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed from a judgment of the County Court in Dutchess County (New York), convicting them of murder in the first degree and criminal possession of a weapon in the second degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were indicted on numerous charges including two counts each of first degree murder. These counts charged each defendant with perpetrating multiple killings in violation of N.Y. Penal Law §125.27(1)(a)(viii). Following separate jury trials, each defendant was convicted of two counts of murder in the first degree and two counts of criminal weapons possession in the second degree. Defendants both appealed alleging that the legislature did not intend to permit first degree murder convictions to rest upon evidence of accessorial liability and that accessorial liability did not provide a constitutionally permissible basis for a charge of first degree murder. The court affirmed the judgment of conviction because the legislature did intend to allow first degree murder convictions to be predicated upon accessorial liability, and accessorial liability was permissible within the strictures of the U.S. Const. amend. VIII under the circumstances of the case.


OUTCOME: Because the legislature intended to allow first degree murder convictions to be predicated upon accessorial liability, and accessorial liability was permissible within the strictures of the Eighth Amendment under the circumstances of the case, the court affirmed the convictions.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Murder - First Degree Murder - Convictions for Multiple Killings Predicated upon Accessorial Liability

1. Defendants' noncapital first degree murder convictions for having perpetrated multiple killings by each shooting one rival drug dealer in a concerted effort to kill off two competitors are lawful and should be affirmed. The murder weapons were traced back to defendants and each defendant's killing of one victim was established beyond a reasonable doubt, even though it could not be established which defendant used which gun. The Legislature clearly intended that the aggravating factor of multiple killings necessary to elevate the offense to first degree murder (Penal Law § 125.27 [1] [a] [viii]) be met by evidence of accessorial liability. The fact that the statute refers to single actors does not preclude an interpretation applying the law to multiple actors (see, General Construction Law § 35). Moreover, the fact that the Legislature expressly limited accessorial liability in first degree felony murder cases so that a nonshooter can be convicted of first degree felony murder only if, during the perpetration of a designated felony, the nonshooter expressly "commanded" the shooter to kill (Penal Law § 125.27 [1] [a] [vii]) does not give rise to the inference that the Legislature intended to limit normal rules of accessorial liability in the multiple killings paragraph of the first degree murder statute. Rather, the correct interpretation of the Legislature's express limitation on accessorial liability in first degree felony murder prosecutions and its failure to do so for multiple killings is that the Legislature did not intend to alter ordinary rules of accomplice liability as applied to multiple killers. Furthermore the legislative history of the statute eliminates any doubt that the Legislature intended to permit first degree murder prosecutions to be brought against accessorially liable defendants, leaving their degree of participation for the determination of the sentencing jury (see, CPL 400.27 [9] [d]).

Crimes - Murder - Constitutionality of First Degree Murder Statute - Convictions for Multiple Killings Predicated upon Accessorial Liability

2. Defendants' noncapital first degree murder convictions for having perpetrated multiple killings by each shooting one rival drug dealer in a concerted effort to kill off two competitors (Penal Law § 125.27 [1] [a] [viii]) are lawful and should be affirmed. The murder weapons were traced back to defendants, and each defendant's killing of one victim was established beyond a reasonable doubt, even though it could not be established which defendant used which gun. There is no constitutional impediment to predicating a charge of first degree murder upon accessorial liability. Accessories who are major participants in intentional killings can be subjected to capital prosecution for their accessorial conduct without running afoul of the Eighth Amendment's proscription against cruel and unusual punishment. Since defendants are clearly major participants and, thus, could have been sentenced to death, their sentences of life without parole cannot be said to violate the Eighth Amendment.

Crimes - Murder - Constitutionality of First Degree Murder Statute - Convictions for Multiple Killings Predicated upon Accessorial Liability - Propriety of Borrowing Statutory Definition of "Criminal Transaction" Used for Double Jeopardy Purposes

3. Defendants' noncapital first degree murder convictions for having perpetrated multiple killings by each shooting one rival drug dealer in a concerted effort to kill off two competitors are lawful and should be affirmed. The murder weapons were traced back to defendants, and each defendant's killing of one victim was established beyond a reasonable doubt, even though it could not be established which defendant used which gun. The first degree murder statute is not unconstitutionally vague and overly broad insofar as it purports to punish multiple killings that are perpetrated as part of the "same criminal transaction" (Penal Law § 125.27 [1] [a] [viii]) without supplying a definition of "same criminal transaction." The statutory definition of "criminal transaction" used for double jeopardy purposes ( CPL 40.10 [2]) may properly be borrowed for application in the first degree murder statute. Consequently, when construed to include the well-settled definition of "criminal transaction" set forth in the double jeopardy statute, the multiple killing provision of the first degree murder statute is clearly sufficiently definite to give an ordinary person fair notice of the proscribed conduct, i.e., multiple homicides closely related in time or criminal purpose, and it provides the requisite standards to avoid ad hoc, subjective determinations and arbitrary application. The facts of this case present multiple and nearly simultaneous shootings by the two defendants, clearly acting in concert to kill two rival drug dealers. Under these circumstances, there is no genuine vagueness problem, since the statute unambiguously proscribes multiple killings, which is specific conduct easily avoided by the innocent-minded.

 

751 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


N.Y State Crime Victims Bd. v. T.J.M. Prods., Inc.


1380


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


265 A.D.2d 38; 705 N.Y.S.2d 320; 2000 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2582; 28 Media L. Rep. 1756



March 7, 2000, Decided  
March 7, 2000, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed from an order of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which granted defendants' motions to dismiss the complaint for failure to state a cause of action under the Son of Sam Law, N.Y. Exec. Law § 632-a.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff, crime victims board (board), sued various defendants under the Son of Sam Law, N.Y. Exec. Law § 632-a. The complaint sought damages, an injunction, and a judgment declaring that the money paid to a Mafia underboss for his participation in the creation of a book and movie constituted "profits of the crime" and that defendants' failure to notify the board of their agreements was a violation of the Son of Sam law. The trial court dismissed the action for failure to state a cause of action, and plaintiff appealed. The court held that a right of action under the Son of Sam Law, granted to the victim of a felony, could not be extended by implication to a crime victims board, whose role was limited to the application for provisional remedies on behalf of a victim who is the plaintiff in an action brought pursuant to the statute.


OUTCOME: Order which granted defendants' motions to dismiss the complaint for failure to state a cause of action under the Son of Sam law was affirmed. Crime victims board could not bring an action in its own right.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Crime Victims Board - Authority to Maintain Action in Absence of Lawsuit by Crime Victim

Plaintiff Crime Victims Board lacks statutory authority to maintain an action under the "Son of Sam" Law (Executive Law § 632-a) for declaratory, injunctive and monetary relief based upon defendants' failure to have turned over the contracts and moneys with respect to a book based upon the life of a well-known former member of an organized crime family who was convicted under the Federal Racketeer Influenced and Corrupt Organizations Act (18 USC § 1962 [c]). The "Son of Sam" Law requires that an entity agreeing to pay any profit from a "crime," defined as "any felony defined in the penal law or any other chapter of the consolidated laws" (Executive Law § 632-a [1] [a]), "to a person charged with or convicted of that crime," notify the Board so that the Board can then notify victims of the crime of the existence of "such profits" (Executive Law § 632-a [2] [b]). The statute, by its express terms, applies only to State crimes and does not encompass a conviction for a Federal offense. Furthermore, the statute bestows the right to bring a civil action to recover moneys identified as profits from the crime exclusively upon the "crime victim" (Executive Law § 632-a [1] [c]). The seminal act that confers authority on the Board to act on behalf of the victim is the receipt of the summons and complaint that has been, or will be, filed by the victim of the crime (Executive Law § 632-a [4], [5] [c]). Only at this juncture may the Board, "acting on behalf of the plaintiff and all other victims," seek any provisional remedy that is available to the plaintiff victim (Executive Law § 632-a [6]). The Board has no implied right to prosecute violations of the notification provision of the "Son of Sam" Law, and even if it were able to establish such an inherent right, the statute provides no penalty for the infraction of this provision.

 

752 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Fraser


(255) KA 99-719.


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


264 A.D.2d 105; 704 N.Y.S.2d 426; 2000 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1837



February 16, 2000, Decided  
February 16, 2000, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a jury verdict from the Oneida County Court convicting him of two counts of possessing a sexual performance by a child N.Y. Penal Law §263.16. Defendant contended that a visual image stored in a computer hard drive was not a "photograph" within the meaning of §263.00(4).


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff state charged defendant with violating N.Y. Penal Law § 263.16 by possessing computer graphic files which contained images of children engaged in sexual conduct. The court held that a visual image stored in a computer hard drive was a "photograph" within the meaning of § 263.00(4) given the legislative intent to eradicate the market for child pornography in all forms. Defendant licensed social worker sought to use the affirmative defense in § 235.15(1), which provided persons having scientific or educational justification for possessing obscene material. This affirmative defense, by its own terms, applied only to obscenity prosecutions and prosecutions under § 235.21(3) and thus did not apply to child pornography. Child pornography was different from general obscenity both in the degree of depravity involved and in the nature and extent of the harm involved.


OUTCOME: The judgment was unanimously affirmed a visual image stored in a computer hard drive was a "photograph" and the affirmative defense permitting possession of obscene materials for educational purposes did not apply to possessing child pornography.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Possessing Sexual Performance by Child - Graphic Images Stored in Computer

1. Defendant, a licensed social worker specializing in treating sexually related disorders, was lawfully convicted of possessing a sexual performance by a child (Penal Law § 263.16) based upon visual images stored in the hard drive of his computer depicting young children engaged in sexual activity with adults. The trial court properly charged the jury that, for purposes of the statute, which defines performance as "any play, motion picture, photograph or dance" (Penal Law § 263.00 [4]), the term "photograph" may include a computer graphic image. Construing the statute as encompassing computer graphic images is consistent with the legislative intent of prohibiting the possession of child pornography in every form as a means of eradicating the market for such material. The graphic images stored in defendant's computer were either photographs that were converted to digital images by a scanner or taken by a digital camera. A graphic image of a child engaged in sexual conduct captures the child's humiliation and preserves it into the future as permanently as does any photograph. The use of computers to transmit and possess child pornography can only increase the market for child pornography, especially with the clarity of images, the speed of transmission, and the ability to upload and download the images with ease.

Crimes - Possessing Sexual Performance by Child - "Scientific" Affirmative Defense

2. Defendant, a licensed social worker specializing in treating sexually related disorders, was lawfully convicted of possessing a sexual performance by a child (Penal Law § 263.16) based upon visual images stored in the hard drive of his computer depicting young children engaged in sexual activity with adults. The trial court properly denied defendant's request to charge the affirmative defense set forth in Penal Law § 235.15, which, inter alia, protects the use of obscene material by persons for scientific, educational or governmental purposes. This affirmative defense, by its own terms, applies only to obscenity prosecutions and prosecutions under Penal Law § 235.21 (3) and thus does not apply here. It is not a violation of equal protection to allow a person who possesses obscene material to assert a "scientific" defense while not allowing a person who possesses child pornography to assert a comparable defense. Child pornography is not protected speech under either the Federal or State Constitutions. The compelling State interest in protecting children from being defiled far outweighs the value of allowing the product of the child's defilement to be used for "scientific" purposes. Moreover, given the nature and extent of the harm to children who are involved in the production of pornography, the compelling interest of the State in eradicating the market for that despicable material and the de minimis value of child pornography for scientific or educational purposes, it is a reasonable determination by the Legislature not to allow a person charged with possession of child pornography to assert the "scientific" defense.

Crimes - Possessing Sexual Performance by Child - Mistake of Law Justification Defense

3. Defendant, a licensed social worker specializing in treating sexually related disorders, was lawfully convicted of possessing a sexual performance by a child (Penal Law § 263.16) based upon visual images stored in the hard drive of his computer depicting young children engaged in sexual activity with adults. Defendant was not entitled to a charge on the mistake of law justification defense set forth in Penal Law § 15.20 (2) (a), which relieves a person of criminal liability if he or she engaged in such conduct in reliance upon an official statement of the law contained in a statute or other enactment. Defendant's contention that Penal Law § 263.00 (4) is ambiguous in defining a performance as, inter alia, a photograph or other visual representation only if exhibited before an audience, and that his mistaken belief that the possession of a computer image was not illegal unless exhibited to an audience was reasonable does not support a charge on the defense of justification. The mistake of law defense was intended to be a very narrow escape valve and does not apply even where a defendant has made a good-faith, even reasonable but mistaken, interpretation of criminal statutes. The defense applies only where an individual demonstrates an effort to learn what the law is, relies on the validity of the law and, later, it is determined that there was a mistake in the law itself. That is not the case here.

 

753 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Port Wash. Union Free Sch. Dist. v. Port Wash. Teachers Ass'n


1998-10300


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


268 A.D.2d 523; 702 N.Y.S.2d 605; 2000 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 705; 164 L.R.R.M. 2435


December 10, 1999, Argued  
January 24, 2000, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, teachers' association (association) and teachers, sought review of an order of the Supreme Court of Nassau County (New York), which granted the motion of respondent school district (district) to stay arbitration pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 7503 after appellants filed a grievance as to the district's refusal to pay teachers for religious holidays and sought to invoke their right to arbitration under a collective bargaining agreement.


OVERVIEW: The collective bargaining agreement between the district and the association contained a provision that teachers could receive any of the religious holidays designated by the New York State Commissioner of Education as a paid day off that would not be charged to any other leave. The district later advised teachers who had requested such days off that it would no longer abide by the provision because it was unconstitutional. Appellants filed a grievance and sought to invoke their contractual right to arbitration. The district brought a proceeding pursuant to Rule 7503 to permanently stay arbitration. The trial court granted the stay, and appellants sought review. The court affirmed, holding that the trial court properly found that the dispute was not subject to arbitration because the provision violated the Establishment Clause (Clause) under the First Amendment. The provision violated the Clause because it rewarded teachers who claimed to be religiously observant with more days off than those afforded to agnostics, atheists, and others who were less observant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order granting a permanent stay of arbitration in the proceeding brought by the district to stay arbitration of appellants' grievance as to paid religious holidays under the collective bargaining agreement.

 

754 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Carty


13-281


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, FIRST DEPARTMENT


55 Misc. 3d 11; 49 N.Y.S.3d 600; 2016 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 5078; 2016 NY Slip Op 26418



 December 14, 2016, Decided


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Disorderly Conduct -- Occupy Wall Street Protest

The evidence established the elements of the two types of disorderly conduct of which defendant was convicted arising from her participation in an Occupy Wall Street protest where the People's proof demonstrated that defendant obstructed pedestrian traffic (see Penal Law § 240.20 [5]) by laying down on a busy Wall Street sidewalk at 4:00 p.m. on a trading day, side-by-side with other Occupy protestors, and refused to comply with a lawful police order to disperse (see Penal Law § 240.20 [6]). Defendant testified that the Occupy demonstrators chose the particular time and location, and to lie down rather than stand, in order to communicate their message about the injustices created by Wall Street "to as many people as possible," and she conceded that she possessed the requisite mens rea to sustain her convictions, i.e., that she acted with intent to cause public inconvenience, annoyance or alarm, or recklessly created a risk thereof. Moreover, the video recording evidence showed that the police placed barricades around the pedestrian area due to the demonstration, that scores of pedestrians were pinned against the barricades and that the police could be heard yelling "keep back" at the crowd, which was precluded from traversing across the sidewalk for fear of trampling the protestors. When conduct, even taking the form of protest, substantially interferes with vehicular or pedestrian traffic, prosecutions for disorderly conduct are appropriate and constitutional.

 

755 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v Westwood (John)


2012-1459 Q CR


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, SECOND DEPARTMENT


53 Misc. 3d 74; 41 N.Y.S.3d 347; 2016 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3425; 2016 NY Slip Op 26303



 September 20, 2016, Decided


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Criminal Trespass -- Facial Sufficiency of Accusatory Instrument

1. The accusatory instrument charging defendant with criminal trespass in the second degree based on allegations that he "wait[ed] at the entrance" of complainant's apartment building "until someone entered the building[,] follow[ed] someone into said building and then walk[ed] up the stairs to the complainant's floor" was facially sufficient, as it provided reasonable cause to believe that defendant "knowingly enter[ed] or remain[ed] unlawfully in a dwelling" (Penal Law § 140.15 [1]). Circumstantial evidence may establish the lack of license or privilege in criminal trespass prosecutions. Thus, the People were not required to negate the possibility that one of the numerous residents of the building invited defendant to enter. Defendant, who, the complainant alleged, did not reside in the building, gained entry not by being "buzzed in" by a resident or another person authorized to do so, but by waiting for someone to enter and following that person into the building. A common area of an apartment building which is separated from the outside by a locked door and buzzer system is part of a "dwelling" as defined in Penal Law § 140.00 (3). The allegations of the nature of defendant's entry into the premises, along with the conduct exhibited following the entry, therefore satisfied the pleading requirements of an information.

Crimes -- Stalking -- Facial Sufficiency of Accusatory Instrument -- Course of Conduct

2. The accusatory instrument charging defendant with stalking in the fourth degree based on allegations that he contacted complainant four times, escalating from a mailed card to an explicit invitation to engage in sexual conduct placed at the door to complainant's apartment, was facially sufficient. It is an element of the offense that defendant intentionally engaged in a course of conduct, meaning a pattern of conduct composed of a series of acts over a period of time evidencing a continuity of purpose. In the stalking context, "continuity of purpose" is not the intent to cause a particular fear of harm but to commit acts which create a reasonable likelihood of fear of harm. The four incidents represented a series of intentional acts united in a continuity of purpose that established a course of conduct directed at complainant with no legitimate purpose. Even if the accusatory instrument did not assert facts upon which, following the first two contacts, a reasonable person would feel threatened, combined with the final two letters which were delivered in so personal a manner, the instrument clearly set forth a course of conduct that defendant should have known was likely to cause reasonable fear of material harm to the complainant's physical health or safety. Although defendant's contact occurred over a nearly two-year period, time was not the controlling factor given the pattern of escalating behavior; rather, it was defendant's persistence as manifested in his ever more personal and transgressive behavior that established a course of conduct.

759 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v Noga (Russell)


2013-2031 P CR


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, SECOND DEPARTMENT


50 Misc. 3d 41; 22 N.Y.S.3d 285; 2015 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3547; 2015 NY Slip Op 25332



 September 18, 2015, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected December 3, 2015.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Ordinance -- Town Code Regulating Parking

In a prosecution of defendant for parking a dump truck at his residential property in violation of Code of the Town of Kent § 77-38 (D) (2), which provides that "[n]ot more than one commercial vehicle, excluding construction equipment, may be parked on any lot in a residential district," the code provision was unconstitutionally vague on its face as it failed to provide adequate notice as to what constitutes a violation. The phrase "excluding construction equipment" was susceptible to multiple meanings--that parking construction equipment in the specified locations is a per se violation of the ordinance, or that construction equipment is completely excluded from the parking prohibition--and did not provide adequate warning as to the nature of the offense prohibited.

 

760 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v Coveney (Mary)


2011-3058 K CR


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, SECOND DEPARTMENT


50 Misc. 3d 1; 21 N.Y.S.3d 523; 2015 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3492; 2015 NY Slip Op 25327



 September 17, 2015, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected December 3, 2015.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Accusatory Instrument -- Facial Sufficiency -- Time Frame -- Continuing Offenses

1. Although defendant failed to preserve the issue for review, an information that charged defendant with, among other offenses, stalking in the fourth degree, harassment in the second degree, and endangering the welfare of a child over a 10-month period was not defective for lack of specificity as it provided defendant with fair notice of the time, place, and manner of the offenses that she was alleged to have committed and a fair opportunity to answer the charges and to prepare a defense. The offenses charged were characterized as continuing offenses since, by definition, they may be committed by multiple acts which occur over a period of time and, as a result, they can be comprised of time spans that might otherwise be considered unreasonably excessive.

Crimes -- Stalking -- Facial Sufficiency of Information

2. An information that charged defendant with stalking in the fourth degree pursuant to Penal Law § 120.45 (1), alleging that defendant repeatedly followed complainant near her home and around the surrounding neighborhood, hid behind trees and bushes near complainant's house, stood in front of and stared at complainant's house, followed complainant's two children who were under the age of 17, and sent letters to complainant's father blaming complainant for adversity in defendant's life, was facially sufficient. While defendant contended that she had a legitimate purpose in pursuing complainant, a school principal, in an effort to get back her job as a substitute teacher, the "no legitimate purpose" element of Penal Law § 120.45 (1) could be inferred from the frequency of the alleged course of conduct. Although defendant was not alleged to have made any verbal threats, the sheer volume of her attempts at communicating with complainant made defendant's course of conduct one which was likely to have caused reasonable fear of material harm to her safety.

Crimes -- Harassment -- Following in Public Place -- Facial Sufficiency of Information

3. An information that charged defendant with harassment in the second degree pursuant to Penal Law § 240.26 (2), alleging, among other things, that defendant repeatedly followed complainant near her home and around the surrounding neighborhood, was facially sufficient as the information alleged that defendant had followed complainant in or about a public place on numerous occasions. Defendant's intent to harass, annoy, or alarm complainant was reasonably inferred from the sheer volume of defendant's attempted unwanted communications, especially after both complainant and the police had requested that such attempts at communication cease.

Crimes -- Harassment -- Physical Contact -- Facial Sufficiency of Information

4. An information that charged defendant with harassment in the second degree pursuant to Penal Law § 240.26 (1), alleging that defendant repeatedly followed complainant near her home and around the surrounding neighborhood, hid behind trees and bushes near complainant's house, stood in front of and stared at complainant's house, followed complainant's two children who were under the age of 17, and sent letters to complainant's father blaming complainant for adversity in defendant's life, was facially insufficient as the factual allegations, standing alone, could not reasonably be construed to represent a genuine and immediate threat of physical contact to complainant.

Crimes -- Harassment -- Course of Conduct -- Sufficiency of Evidence at Trial

5. While an information that charged defendant with harassment in the second degree pursuant to Penal Law § 240.26 (3), alleging, among other things, that defendant repeatedly sent letters to complainant's father blaming complainant for adversity in defendant's life, was facially sufficient, defendant's conviction was vacated where the People offered three letters writ ten to complainant's father into evidence but he did not testify at the trial. Without the letters in evidence, and with little testimony elicited from complainant pertaining to them, the People failed to prove that, by sending these letters to complainant's father, defendant had engaged in a course of conduct intended to harass, annoy, or alarm complainant.

Crimes -- Attempt -- Endangering Welfare of Child -- Sufficiency of Evidence at Trial

6. While an information that charged defendant with two counts of attempted endangering the welfare of a child pursuant to Penal Law §§110.00 and 260.10 (1), alleging that defendant, among other things, followed complainant's two children who were under the age of 17, was facially sufficient, defendant's conviction was vacated where no nexus was established by the People at trial between defendant's conduct and the likelihood of injury toward complainant's children. Complainant only testified to one occasion when defendant followed one of her sons "parallel across the street down the block," which event occurred before the dates of the offending conduct alleged in the information. The People failed to prove the element of Penal Law § 260.10 (1) that defendant knowingly acted in a manner likely to be injurious to the physical, mental or moral welfare of complainant's children.

People v. LaRoche


2010-3314 N CR


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, SECOND DEPARTMENT


44 Misc. 3d 20; 988 N.Y.S.2d 839; 2014 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2307; 2014 NY Slip Op 24133



May 7, 2014


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected June 12, 2015.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Ordinance -- On-Site Inspection as Condition of Obtaining Rental Occupancy Permit

North Hempstead Town Code § 2-106, which provides for an on-site inspection as a condition of obtaining a rental occupancy permit, is facially constitutional. Although Code § 2-106 requires an inspection of the premises as a condition of the issuance of a rental permit, and Code § 2-110 authorizes the code enforcement officer to enter the premises upon consent (see Code § 2-103), Code § 2-111 provides for an application to the District Court by a code enforcement officer for a search warrant upon a showing of reasonable cause to believe that code violations exist. A code provision which expressly requires either the consent of the property owner or the issuance of a valid search warrant on reasonable grounds to believe that a violation of the local code exists is facially constitutional.

People v LaRoche


2010-3314 N CR


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, SECOND DEPARTMENT


13 N.Y.S.3d 773; 2014 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 6001; 2014 NY Slip Op 24133
