764 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Novie


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, SECOND DEPARTMENT


41 Misc. 3d 63; 976 N.Y.S.2d 636; 2013 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4161; 2013 NY Slip Op 23315



September 17, 2013, Decided


HEADNOTES

Local Laws -- Validity -- Tree Preservation Law

1. The Tree Preservation and Landscape Maintenance Law of the Code of the Village of Montebello (Tree Law), which contained the provisions under which defendant was charged for having cut down trees on his property that he allegedly did not have a permit to remove (Code of Village of Montebello §§ 176-6 [A] [1]; 176-7 [C]), is not ultra vires. The stated legislative intent behind the Tree Law is to preserve trees, provide appropriate aesthetic streetscapes, and regulate and prevent conditions which cause increased surface runoff, soil erosion, and decreased soil fertility (Code of Village of Montebello § 176-2). General Municipal Law § 96-b, which specifically allows for the adoption of tree preservation laws, supports that intent as it recognizes that the destructive and indiscriminate removal of trees and related vegetation causes increased municipal costs for proper drainage control, impairs the benefits of occupancy of existing residential properties and impairs the stability and value of both improved and unimproved real property in the area of destruction. Moreover, an ordinance can have the purpose of promoting aesthetic considerations which bear substantially on the economic, social and cultural community. Furthermore, the Tree Law is not ultra vires based on the fees associated with the law. The power of a village to charge fees must be reasonably necessary to the accomplishment of the regulatory program. Here, not only does the Tree Law properly require reimbursement of the professional consultant fees paid by the village in connection with the review of a tree cutting application, it also provides for an audit procedure for the fees.

Constitutional Law -- Taking of Property -- Ripeness Doctrine

2. Defendant's claim that the provisions of the Tree Preservation and Landscape Maintenance Law of the Code of the Village of Montebello (Tree Law), under which he was charged for having cut down trees on his property that he allegedly did not have a permit to remove (Code of Village of Montebello §§ 176-6 [A] [1]; 176-7 [C]), resulted in a taking of his property without compensation was not ripe for review. A claim that the application of a government ordinance effects a taking of a property interest is not ripe until the government entity charged with implementing the ordinance has reached a final decision regarding the application of the ordinance to the property at issue. Tree Law § 176-6 (B) (2) and (D) delineate the process by which a property owner can seek a retroactive application for a permit to remove trees and an exception to any requirement of chapter 176, and Code of the Village of Montebello § 65-6 provides the process for an applicant to dispute the amount of consultant fees charged by the village. Here, the record revealed that defendant started the procedure to obtain an exception to the requirements of chapter 176, but thereafter abandoned his application, which application was not approved or denied. Consequently, since defendant failed to fully pursue an exception to the Tree Law, and given the fact that he did not show that there was a final decision, in that the process could be reinstated and continued, his takings claim was not ripe. Defendant also failed to show that he had sought compensation through state procedures providing for same and had not received compensation. Furthermore, defendant's claim that the consultant fees of the Tree Law resulted in a taking of his property without due process of law was not ripe for review inasmuch as chapter 65 of the Code of the Village of Montebello clearly provides for adequate process, which defendant, admittedly, chose not to pursue.

Local Laws -- Validity -- Tree Preservation Law -- Due Process Clause

3. The Tree Preservation and Landscape Maintenance Law of the Code of the Village of Montebello (Tree Law), which contained the provisions under which defendant was charged for having cut down trees on his property that he allegedly did not have a permit to remove (Code of Village of Montebello §§ 176-6 [A] [1]; 176-7 [C]), was reasonable and did not constitute a deprivation of property without due process of law. Tree Law § 176-6 (A) (1) delineates what a person cannot do, with respect to trees, on privately owned property, and Tree Law § 176-6 (B) delineates the permitted activities on the property, and provides when a property owner must obtain a permit to remove a tree. Moreover, the Tree Law was enacted for the legitimate governmental purpose of preserving trees, providing appropriate aesthetic streetscapes, and regulating and preventing conditions which cause increased surface runoff, soil erosion, and decreased soil fertility (see Tree Law § 176-2), and is supported by the home rule grant of authority. Furthermore, there is a reasonable relationship between the ends sought to be achieved by the ordinance and the means used (i.e., the permit and fee process) to achieve that end.

Local Laws -- Validity -- Tree Preservation Law -- Equal Protection Clause

4. Sections 176-6 (A) (1) and 176-7 (C) of the Tree Preservation and Landscape Maintenance Law of the Code of the Village of Montebello (Tree Law), under which defendant was charged for having cut down trees on his property that he did not have a permit to remove, did not violate defendant's equal protection rights. The legislative intent behind the Tree Law--preserving trees, providing appropriate aesthetic streetscapes, and regulating and preventing conditions which cause increased surface runoff, soil erosion, and decreased soil fertility--advances a valid public policy basis for affecting some groups differently than others, i.e., those owning property in the Village of Montebello prior to the implementation of the Tree Law and those purchasing property in the village after its implementation. Moreover, defendant failed to show that he was intentionally treated differently from others similarly situated and that there was no rational basis for the difference in treatment.

 

765 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. On Sight Mobile Opticians


2012-384 S CR.


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, SECOND DEPARTMENT


40 Misc. 3d 95; 971 N.Y.S.2d 656; 2013 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2933; 2013 NY Slip Op 23235



July 8, 2013, Decided


HEADNOTES

Local Laws -- Validity -- Constitutionality of Town Law Regulating Signs

Chapter 57A of the Town of Brookhaven Code, which contained the provision under which defendant was convicted for having placed signs advertising its business on public property (Town of Brookhaven Code § 57A-11), is unconstitutional. Zoning ordinances will be upheld if there is a reasonable relation between the end sought to be achieved and the means adopted to achieve it. Time, place and manner restrictions are permissible if they are justified without reference to the content of the regulated speech, serve a significant governmental interest, and leave open ample alternative channels for the communication of the information. Here, the stated purpose of chapter 57A, which was essentially to avoid unsightly proliferation of unnecessary signs in the interests of traffic safety and esthetics, represented a substantial government interest which was constitutionally furthered by section 57A-11. Although the chapter barred commercial advertising on public lands and roads, it provided ample opportunity to advertise commercial activities on private land and was thus sufficiently narrowly tailored. In addition, the law was not unconstitutionally vague as the terms employed were either specifically defined in the chapter or amenable to the commonsense understanding. However, chapter 57A unconstitutionally favored commercial speech over noncommercial speech. The chapter's language of limitation, "only the following signs" are "permitted" or "allowed," imposed broad restrictions on noncommercial speech that was not "political." Further, commercial advertising was permitted in every zoning district aside from public lands and roads while noncommercial speech was barred in most contexts in which commercial speech was allowed. Given the use of the language of limitation and the relative absence of media of expression for noncommercial speech, it was impossible to sever the unconstitutional portions of chapter 57A.

 

766 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Carboy (John)


2011-3 N CR.


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, SECOND DEPARTMENT


37 Misc. 3d 83; 955 N.Y.S.2d 473; 2012 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4498; 2012 NY Slip Op 22266



September 13, 2012, Decided


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Stalking -- Sufficiency of Evidence 

1. The verdict convicting defendant of stalking in the fourth degree (Penal Law § 120.45 [2]) based on defendant's actions, after being told by a police detective to cease all contact and communication with the complainant, in making gestures to provoke and aggravate complainant and producing and distributing T-shirts displaying degrading pictures of the complainant accompanied by derogatory, vulgar or suggestive comments was not against the weight of the evidence. The complainant described how defendant's actions and the existence of the T-shirts caused material harm to her mental and emotional health. Moreover, defendant, at the time of his arrest, stated that he knew the complainant would learn of the T-shirts and would get angry.

Crimes -- Stalking -- Constitutionality of Statute -- Producing and Distributing T-Shirts with Degrading Pictures of and Comments About Complainant 

2. Penal Law § 120.45 (2) was not unconstitutionally overbroad as applied to the stalking in the fourth degree prosecution of defendant for producing and distributing four T-shirts displaying degrading pictures of the complainant accompanied by derogatory, vulgar or suggestive comments after being told by a police detective to cease all contact and communication with the complainant. The statute did not impermissibly punish defendant's use of photographs of the complainant or improperly punish his constitutionally protected speech. A defendant's right to free speech is permissibly subordinated to the complainant's right to be free of repetitive, unwanted verbal and nonverbal communications which are likely to instill in the complainant a reasonable fear of harm. Here, defendant unacceptably intruded upon the complainant's privacy rights, and his unwanted nonverbal communications inflicted immeasurable emotional harm upon the complainant, which conduct was precisely what the stalking statute intended to criminalize.

 

767 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v Romano (Stacey)


570183/06


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, FIRST DEPARTMENT


29 Misc. 3d 9; 908 N.Y.S.2d 520; 2010 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4052; 2010 NY Slip Op 20344



August 24, 2010, Decided


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Animal Cruelty Statute Not Unconstitutionally Vague

1. Agriculture and Markets Law § 353 was not unconstitutionally vague on its face or as applied to defendant, who was charged with failing to groom her dog for a prolonged period of time and failing to seek medical care, as the phrase "unjustifiably injures" is sufficiently definite to give a person of ordinary intelligence fair notice that conduct such as that engaged in by defendant was proscribed by the statute, and provided officials with clear standards of enforcement. A citizen can readily comprehend that he or she must refrain from causing unjustifiable injury to a domestic pet by failing to groom the animal for several months and to seek medical care for the animal when clear, objective signs are present that the animal needs such care. Moreover, in light of the significant physical maladies the dog developed as a result of defendant's neglect and the odor the dog emitted because of those maladies, no reasonable pet owner would fail to seek medical care for the animal.

Animals -- Cruelty to Animals -- Failure to Groom and Provide Medical Care to Dog

2. Legally sufficient evidence existed to establish defendant's guilt of animal cruelty and falsely reporting an incident in the third degree where the trial evidence, including the testimony of the experienced veterinarian who treated defendant's dog, established that the dog had not been groomed for a prolonged period of time causing the dog to develop medical problems which defendant failed to seek medical care for despite clear, objective signs that the dog needed medical attention. Furthermore, the evidence established that defendant, in a written statement voluntarily provided to the law enforcement officer investigating the dog's condition, falsely reported both that another person had custody of the dog in the three-month period preceding the date on which defendant signed the statement and that the dog's maladies developed during that period.

 

768 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v Hoffstead (Eric)


2007-943 W CR.


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, SECOND DEPARTMENT


28 Misc. 3d 16; 905 N.Y.S.2d 736; 2010 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1572; 2010 NY Slip Op 20226



June 10, 2010, Decided


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Loitering -- Loitering for Purpose of Begging -- Constitutionality of Statute 

1. Penal Law § 240.35 (1), which provides that a person is guilty of loitering if he or she loiters, remains or wanders about in a public place for the purpose of begging, is unconstitutional because it violates the freedom of speech guarantee of the First Amendment. Begging is as inseparable from speech as is charitable solicitation and is subject to the same level of First Amendment scrutiny. Even if the People may assert the State's interests in the statute for the first time on appeal, the asserted interests in maintaining public order, reducing citizen fear of coercive encounters with panhandlers, and preventing fraud could be sufficiently served by measures less destructive of First Amendment interests than the sweeping prohibition of all begging in all public places at all times that Penal Law § 240.35 (1) imposes.

Crimes -- Harassment -- Intent 

2. The testimony of the arresting officer at defendant's suppression hearing did not demonstrate the existence of probable cause for defendant's arrest for harassment in the second degree (Penal Law § 240.26 [2]). The officer testified that defendant had "followed a . . . young male Hispanic . . . for several blocks"; that the person then handed defendant what appeared to be a pack of cigarettes; and that the person subsequently told the arresting officer that defendant had asked him for money and cigarettes, and told another police officer that he had become "nervous" and given defendant a pack of cigarettes. An intent to "harass, annoy or alarm" may be inferred from the conduct of a defendant, but the arresting officer's testimony fell short of establishing that defendant harbored the requisite "intent to harass, annoy or alarm" when he "follow[ed]" the "young male Hispanic," who was not a witness at the hearing. The officer did not testify that defendant had any interaction with that person while following him, or that defendant followed him so closely as to appear threatening. Although the person allegedly became "nervous" when defendant spoke to him, that fact did not prove that defendant had any intent to "harass, annoy or alarm" him.

 

769 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Laroche


2008-1111 N CR


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, SECOND DEPARTMENT


27 Misc. 3d 97; 902 N.Y.S.2d 878; 2010 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 823; 2010 NY Slip Op 20142



April 13, 2010, Decided


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Ordinance -- Failing to Obtain Rental Occupancy Permit 

1. Defendant failed to demonstrate the unconstitutionality of the municipal ordinance under which she was convicted for failing to obtain a rental occupancy permit before renting out her dwelling for residential occupancy. Since the town code otherwise provides for the inspection of residential dwellings upon consent and pursuant to a warrant, the challenged code provision does not unconstitutionally compel a residential property owner to consent to a warrantless search of the property.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Ordinance -- Erecting Building Structure without Permit 

2. Defendant failed to demonstrate that a town ordinance, which makes it unlawful to erect, alter or maintain a building structure without permits, was unconstitutionally vague. Defendant failed to rebut the strong presumption in favor of the ordinance's constitutionality. The ordinance clearly and unequivocally put defendant on notice of the proscribed activity by informing her that it is unlawful to erect, alter or maintain any building or structure for which a permit is required until such a permit has been issued. It is a valid content-neutral ordinance of general applicability, adopted to promote the health, safety and general welfare of the public.

Municipal Corporations -- Permits -- Erecting, Altering or Maintaining Building Structure

3. The evidence in a nonjury trial was legally insufficient to sustain defendant's conviction on one of four counts of an accusatory instrument charging her with violating a town ordinance by erecting, altering or maintaining a building structure without the required permits. The testimony of the town inspector, that he had seen a "three piece bathroom" in the basement of the property, without explaining what specifically he had observed, was insufficient to establish defendant's guilt beyond a reasonable doubt with regard to the third count. However, the inspector's testimony was sufficient to establish defendant's guilt on the other three counts involving construction of a fence, garage bedrooms and a basement kitchen. Furthermore, the evidence was legally insufficient to establish defendant's guilt on two of four counts involving a second building she owned. The inspector's testimony that he had observed two wall air conditioner units on this building without explaining how the building had been altered was insufficient to establish beyond a reasonable doubt defendant's guilt of erecting, altering or maintaining a building or structure without a permit. The inspector's testimony was, however, sufficient to establish defendant's guilt with respect to the remaining two counts involving a fence and a deck roof.

Municipal Corporations -- Permits -- Rental Occupancy Permit 

4. The evidence in a nonjury trial was legally sufficient to sustain defendant's conviction on one of two counts of an accusatory instrument charging her with violating a town ordinance by failing to obtain a rental occupancy permit for her property. The town inspector's testimony was sufficient to establish defendant's guilt beyond a reasonable doubt in that there were separate entrances for segregated parts of the dwelling, and the dwelling had partitions or internal doors which might serve to bar access between segregated portions of the dwelling. However, since the People did not establish that a rental permit was needed for each purported separate rental unit inside of a building, the proof was sufficient to find defendant guilty of only one count.

 

770 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v Beck (Jonathan)


570357/06, 570358/06, 570359/06, 570360/06, 570361/06, 570362/06, 570363/06


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, FIRST DEPARTMENT


26 Misc. 3d 42; 894 N.Y.S.2d 318; 2010 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3; 2010 NY Slip Op 20000



January 4, 2010, Decided


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Parading without Permit 

In the prosecution of defendants, participants in a "Critical Mass" bicycle ride, for parading without a permit in violation of Administrative Code of the City of NY § 10-110, the permit law under which defendants were convicted was unconstitutional on its face because it failed to adequately define those events that required a permit (see 38 RCNY 19-02 [former (a)]). At the time defendants were arrested and convicted, a "parade or procession" was defined as "any march, motorcade, caravan, promenade, foot or bicycle race, or similar event of any kind, upon any public street or roadway" (38 RCNY 19-02 [former (a)]). As it applied to essentially any group of people moving on a public street, including small groups, it burdened substantially more speech and expressive conduct than was necessary to further the City's legitimate interests in crowd and traffic control. Moreover, the permit law under which defendants were convicted afforded the City, acting through the police commissioner, unduly broad discretion in determining whether a particular event required a permit.

People v. Quiroga-Puma

HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Proof of Identity Requirements for Obtaining Driver's License -- Undocumented Aliens

The trial court erroneously dismissed, sua sponte, the accusatory instrument charging defendant with unlicensed operation of a motor vehicle on the ground that the licensing statutes (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 502 [1]; § 509 [1]), which require identity documentation as a condition precedent to issuance of a driver's license, are unconstitutional as applied to defendant, a noncitizen. The constitutional issues were not before the court given the absence of evidence that the defendant had standing to advance the issues. The record did not contain any indication that defendant was an undocumented alien, had applied for and been denied a license, or had been denied a license on statutory, regulatory or constitutional grounds. Assuming defendant had standing, the court failed to provide the Attorney General with the requisite notice and opportunity to intervene.

HEADNOTES

Motor Vehicles -- Traffic Infractions -- Mandatory Surcharge -- Riding Bicycle on Sidewalk 

In a prosecution for riding a bicycle on a sidewalk where there was no sign permitting it (Administrative Code of City of NY § 19-176 [b]), the court erred in imposing a mandatory surcharge upon defendant. A violation of that section is a traffic infraction; however Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1809, which governs mandatory surcharges for traffic infractions, specifically excludes traffic infractions involving bicyclists.

774 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Jones


570375/05.


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, FIRST DEPARTMENT


18 Misc. 3d 63; 852 N.Y.S.2d 567; 2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 7689; 2007 NY Slip Op 27482; 238 N.Y.L.J. 106



November 23, 2007, Decided


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Jurisdiction of Offenses -- Criminal Division of Supreme Court in Bronx County 

Supreme Court properly acquired jurisdiction over the prosecution of defendant for attempted criminal contempt in the second degree, which was initiated in Criminal Court by misdemeanor information and transferred to Criminal Division of the Supreme Court in Bronx County pursuant to 22 NYCRR 142.2, notwithstanding that it was not initiated by indictment or Supreme Court information as specified in CPL 210.05. CPL 210.05 circumscribes a prosecutor's ability to invoke a superior court's jurisdiction and cannot be read as a limitation on Supreme Court's historical and constitutional power to preside over misdemeanor cases. There is no constitutional requirement that a defendant charged with a misdemeanor be prosecuted by indictment. Further, an information may serve in misdemeanor cases as a basis both for the commencement and prosecution of a criminal action. Thus, there is no statutory impediment to the transfer of misdemeanor cases from Criminal Court to Supreme Court, particularly given the express recognition in the Criminal Procedure Law that Supreme Court has trial jurisdiction over misdemeanors concurrent with that of the local criminal courts (CPL 10.20 [1]), and that Criminal Court's jurisdiction over misdemeanors is subject to divestiture by a superior court in any particular case (CPL 10.30 [1] [b]).

 

775 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Avgush v. Berrahu


2006-870 W C.


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, SECOND DEPARTMENT


17 Misc. 3d 85; 847 N.Y.S.2d 343; 2007 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 6952; 2007 NY Slip Op 27424



October 10, 2007, Decided


HEADNOTES

Landlord and Tenant -- Summary Proceedings -- Default Money Judgment Allowed Where Service Effected in Manner Sufficient for Entry of Money Judgment in Plenary Action 

In a nonpayment summary proceeding, wherein petitioner served the notice of petition and petition on respondent tenants by affixing the papers to the door of the premises and mailing the papers by certified and first-class mail after having made five attempts over two days to personally deliver the papers, petitioner was entitled to a default money judgment for rent arrears. A money judgment is available upon a tenant's default in a summary proceeding, without regard to the manner of service effected therein, upon a showing that such service is sufficient to support the entry of a money judgment in a plenary action. The "due diligence" required under CPLR 308 (4) before resort may be had to conspicuous place service provides a service procedure sufficient to meet constitutional due process requirements, and requires a higher level of effort than the "reasonable application" standard set forth in RPAPL 735 (1) for service in summary proceedings. Moreover, allowing default money judgments upon substituted service in summary proceedings advances the interest of judicial economy and discourages tenants from evading personal delivery. Here, petitioner's efforts at personal delivery satisfied both the "reasonable application" and "due diligence" standards.

 

776 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Little


2005-263 K CR


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, SECOND DEPARTMENT


14 Misc. 3d 70; 830 N.Y.S.2d 428; 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3921; 2006 NY Slip Op 26522



December 13, 2006, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment of conviction affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Aggravated Harassment 

1. The second degree aggravated harassment statute (Penal Law § 240.30 [1] [a]) was not unconstitutional as applied to defendant, who telephoned complainant's house numerous times stating that he would take away her child and ruin her financially, and calling her a bitch and slut. Penal Law § 240.30 (1) (a) prohibits communications with a person by telephone in a "manner likely to cause annoyance or alarm." As applied to defendant, the statute was properly directed to communications made to an unwilling recipient wherein substantial privacy interests were invaded in an essentially intolerable manner. The behavior alleged fell squarely into the core proscription of the aggravated harassment statute. Thus, the statute did not unconstitutionally limit defendant's free speech.

Crimes -- Aggravated Harassment 

2. In a prosecution for attempted aggravated harassment in the second degree (Penal Law § 240.30 [1] [a]) wherein defendant telephoned complainant's house numerous times stating that he would take away her child and ruin her financially, and calling her a bitch and slut, the evidence was legally sufficient to establish defendant's guilt beyond a reasonable doubt and the verdict was not against the weight of the evidence. The evidence showed that defendant engaged in conduct which tended to effect the commission of the crime in that he intended to harass, annoy, threaten or alarm the complainant by communicating with her by telephone in a manner likely to cause her annoyance or alarm. Defendant's phone calls were an unwanted invasion of complainant's privacy. No public interest was involved, since defendant's messages were made to a private citizen on her private telephone line. Proof of a physical threat was not required, since it is not an element of Penal Law § 240.30 (1).

 

777 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Anthony


2004-1533 N CR.


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, SECOND DEPARTMENT


14 Misc. 3d 54; 830 N.Y.S.2d 629; 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3691; 2006 NY Slip Op 26494



November 30, 2006, Decided


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Appeal -- Waiver of Right to Appeal -- Challenge to Constitutionality of Statute Not Foreclosed -- Failure to Raise Constitutional Issue on Direct Appeal 

Defendant's motion pursuant to CPL 440.10 to vacate his conviction of renting premises without first obtaining rental permits in violation of section 2-103 of the Code of the Town of North Hempstead was properly denied even though sections 2-104 (B) (3) and 2-113 of the ordinance requiring an owner to disclose the names, ages and relationships of the occupants as part of the permit process were declared unconstitutional as violative of the tenants' right to privacy in an appellate ruling in defendant's declaratory judgment action eight months after defendant had pleaded guilty to the charges. In the declaratory judgment action the Appellate Division did not rule on the constitutionality of section 2-103 because that issue had not been raised in the court below. Defendant had raised the constitutionality of section 2-103 in the criminal action prior to pleading guilty and the court abstained from ruling on it. Although in pleading guilty defendant waived his right to appeal, such guilty plea and waiver did not foreclose defendant's right to appeal on the ground that the statute that he violated was unconstitutional. Defendant failed to take a direct appeal from the judgments of conviction, in which he could have raised the constitutionality of section 2-103 regardless of the procedural posture of the declaratory judgment action. He thereby forfeited review of that issue. Under these circumstances, the declaratory judgment action and the subsequent appeal had no bearing on the disposition of defendant's case.

 

778 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Lebrecht


2005-851 W CR.


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, SECOND DEPARTMENT


13 Misc. 3d 45; 823 N.Y.S.2d 824; 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2287; 2006 NY Slip Op 26347



July 27, 2006, Decided


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Right of Confrontation -- Admissibility of Breath Test Foundational Documents

1. In defendant's prosecution for driving while impaired (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1192 [1]) and failure to use a designated lane (Vehicle & Traffic Law §1128 [c]), the certified copies of the simulator solution certification and the calibration/maintenance record of the breath test instrument used by police officers to test defendant's blood alcohol content (BAC) were properly admitted as business records as part of the foundation requirements for proof of the results of the analysis of the alcohol content of defendant's blood. The certification records were not testimonial in nature and could thus be admitted as business records without violating defendant's right of confrontation. The certifications were prepared in the course of the certifiers' routine official duties and did not result from structured police questioning or any official request to gather incriminating evidence. The certifications did not contain formalized testimonial materials but merely served an administrative function to ensure properly functioning BAC testing machines.

Crimes -- Evidence -- Admissibility of Arresting Officers' Police Reports

2. In defendant's prosecution for driving while impaired (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1192 [1]) and failure to use a designated lane (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1128 [c]) resulting from an accident when defendant swerved from her traffic lane while trying to retrieve a dropped cell phone, the arresting officers' police reports were properly admitted into evidence as business records with regard to the officers' personal observations of defendant's behavior. Morever, since the trial court sustained defendant's objection to so much of the reports' contents as constituted hearsay and evidence cumulative of other testimony, and in view of the overwhelming proof of guilt, any error in admitting the reports was harmless. The officers' account of their personal experience and police training with respect to the effects of alcohol on behavior sufficed to enable the trial court to properly evaluate their opinions as to the degree of defendant's impairment as a result of alcohol consumption.

Motor Vehicles -- Chemical Tests -- Admissibility of Blood Alcohol Content Test Results -- "Continuous Observation" Requirement

3. In defendant's prosecution for driving while impaired (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1192 [1]) and failure to use a designated lane (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1128 [c]), the trial court properly admitted the results of the chemical test of defendant's blood alcohol content (BAC), notwithstanding the fact that the officer who administered the test did not maintain a "continuous observation" of defendant for 15 minutes prior to the test (10 NYCRR 59.5) during the few minutes his attention was devoted to preparing the BAC machine for testing. There was no evidence that the officer failed to observe any event that would have undermined the accuracy of the test results. In any event, the "continuous observation" requirement should not be strictly construed. "Continuous observation" is not a condition precedent for the test results' admission, but merely goes to the weight to be afforded the test results.

781 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Peak Carting, Inc.


2005-442 S CR


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, SECOND DEPARTMENT


11 Misc. 3d 4; 812 N.Y.S.2d 218; 2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2880; 2005 NY Slip Op 25546



December 19, 2005, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment of conviction unanimously affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Local Ordinance Prohibiting Dumping of Debris 

Defendant was lawfully convicted of dumping debris, consisting of bramble and brush, on premises that were not an approved disposal facility in violation of a town ordinance that prohibited "debris of any sort or any other organic or inorganic material" from being "dumped, deposited or otherwise disposed of or upon any . . . area whether publically or privately owned" except at a public disposal facility or a private disposal facility licensed by the Town. Defendant failed to demonstrate that the ordinance was facially unconstitutional. The Town possessed the authority to impose reasonable limitations on the dumping of waste within the town (see NY Const, art IX, § 2 [c]; Municipal Home Rule Law § 10 [1] [i], [ii]). In addition, the State's regulation of solid waste management did not preempt the Town from enacting regulations that were at least equally stringent (see ECL 27-0711). Furthermore, the ordinance was not unconstitutionally vague as applied to defendant. The ordinance clearly apprised a person of ordinary intelligence that the dumping of bramble and brush on other than licensed facilities constituted a violation.

 

782 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hodge


2476/04


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, FIRST DEPARTMENT


10 Misc. 3d 456; 802 N.Y.S.2d 613; 2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2293; 2005 NY Slip Op 25440



October 18, 2005, Decided


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Double Jeopardy -- Retrial on Lesser Included Count Following Acquittal on Higher Count

In the prosecution of defendant arising out of the recovery of a loaded firearm from his vehicle, defendant's acquittal on the first count of the indictment charging criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree (Penal Law § 265.02 [4]) precluded his retrial on the lesser included count of criminal possession of a weapon in the fourth degree upon which the jury was unable to reach a verdict (see CPL 310.70 [2]). The only issue presented to the jury was whether defendant had knowing possession of the loaded firearm at the time and place charged. The only rational basis for the acquittal verdict on the higher count was the jury's unanimous conclusion that the People's evidence in the form of the integrated testimony of a single police witness failed to prove beyond a reasonable doubt defendant's knowing possession of the weapon in question. Under these circumstances, collateral estoppel and double jeopardy principles precluded defendant's retrial on the second count of the indictment. Moreover, defendant's waiver resulting from the failure to object to the submission of the second count to the jury (see CPL 300.50 [1]) did not survive the first trial as to permit an otherwise unconstitutional retrial on that count.

784 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Aijaz v. Hillside Place, LLC


2004-821 Q C


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, SECOND DEPARTMENT


8 Misc. 3d 73; 798 N.Y.S.2d 840; 2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 860; 2005 NY Slip Op 25164



April 29, 2005, Decided



DISPOSITION: As so modified, affirmed without costs.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and Tenant -- Rent Regulation -- Preferential Rent - Renewal Lease 

In an action seeking damages for rent overcharges in which plaintiff rent-stabilized tenant alleged that defendant landlord collected the full legal regulated rent even though the lease provided for a lower, preferential rent in consideration for plaintiff's relocation, which preferential rent was to continue during the renewal periods, defendant was entitled to assert an affirmative defense against the cause of action for breach of the rent overcharge statutes, but not an affirmative defense against the cause of action for common-law breach of contract. A 2003 amendment to the Rent Stabilization Law of 1969 permitted an owner to discontinue a preferential rent and resume charging the legal regulated rent upon renewal (see Administrative Code of City of NY § 26-511 [c] [14]). As preferential rents are now revocable, the preferential rent listed in the parties' lease did not become the legal regulated or authorized rent, and a collection of rent in excess of the preferential rent constituted only a breach of the agreement of the parties and not an offense against the rent overcharge statutes.

People v. Lord


2004-652 N CR


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, SECOND DEPARTMENT


7 Misc. 3d 78; 796 N.Y.S.2d 511; 2005 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 300; 2005 NY Slip Op 25066



February 22, 2005, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order of the Justice Court reversed on the law; Defendant's motion to dismiss denied, information reinstated and matter remanded for all further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Noise Ordinance 

In the prosecution of defendant for violation of a local noise ordinance, Justice Court erred in holding that Village of Westbury Code § 168-3 (A) is unconstitutionally vague. The "reasonability" and "reasonable person" standard employed in the ordinance's definition of a "noise disturbance" (Village of Westbury Code § 168-2) could be interpreted on the basis of common-life experience, and served to render enforcement of the ordinance more, not less, predictable. There is no constitutional requirement that a decibel meter or other such device be used to determine whether a noise level will be considered illegal.

Rudgayzer & Gratt v. LRS Communications, Inc.


2003-1809 K C


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, SECOND DEPARTMENT


6 Misc. 3d 20; 789 N.Y.S.2d 601; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1977; 2004 NY Slip Op 24420



October 29, 2004, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order of the Civil Court unanimously modified so as to delete the provision imposing costs upon Plaintiff's counsel; as so modified, affirmed without costs.


HEADNOTES

Actions -- Class Actions -- Recovery of Penalty under Telephone Consumer Protection Act 

No class action under the Telephone Consumer Protection Act of 1991 (47 USC § 227 [TCPA]) is available in the New York State courts. The TCPA itself cedes authority to accept, decline, and otherwise define jurisdiction over private TCPA actions to the states. CPLR 901 (b) bars a class action to recover a statutory penalty, such as that provided for by the TCPA, unless the statute providing for the penalty specifically authorizes such a class action. The TCPA does not specifically authorize class actions. Thus, since the TCPA cedes jurisdictional authority over private causes of actions to the states, and CPLR 901 (b) prohibits class actions for statutory penalties unless the statute specifically provides for such actions, plaintiff's proposed TCPA class action was not permissible in the New York courts.

People v. Bull


570750/02


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, FIRST DEPARTMENT


5 Misc. 3d 39; 784 N.Y.S.2d 270; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1280



August 11, 2004, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment of conviction affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes--Loitering--Loitering while Masked or Disguised--Constitutionality of Penal Law § 240.35 (4) 

1. Penal Law § 240.35 (4), which prohibits being masked or disguised in a public place while congregating with others who are also masked or disguised, does not infringe upon any First Amendment right to anonymous speech or constitute impermissible viewpoint discrimination. Nor is the statute unconstitutionally overbroad or vague.

Crimes--Loitering--Loitering while Masked or Disguised

2. In the prosecution of defendants for loitering in violation of Penal Law § 240.35 (4), the trial court properly convicted defendants upon proof, unchallenged on either sufficiency or weight of the evidence grounds, that they and others gathered next to each other "as a group" and covered their faces with bandanas while shouting epithets and political slogans during a May Day demonstration in a public park. The trial court did not improperly shift the burden of proof from the People to defendants on any recognized element of the loitering offense. The trial court's in limine ruling, which allowed defendants to introduce evidence as to the nonstatutory "legitimate purpose" defense erroneously carved out by the motion court, did not ease the People's trial burden or cause defendants any discernible prejudice. Nor was it necessary for the underlying informations to allege the inapplicability of the "masquerade party or like entertainment" exemption contained in Penal Law § 240.35 (4). The masquerade party exemption is a proviso to be raised as a defense by a defendant, rather than an exception to be pleaded and proved by the People.

 

789 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Rudgayzer & Gratt v. Enine, Inc.


NO. 2002-1700 K C, NO. 2002-1740 K C


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, SECOND DEPARTMENT


4 Misc. 3d 4; 779 N.Y.S.2d 882; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 420



April 14, 2004, Decided



DISPOSITION: In Action No. 1 (Enine Inc. case): order reversed; plaintiff's motion for summary judgment granted as to liability; matter remanded for an assessment of damages.

In Action No. 2 (Bonime case): order reversed; defendant's motion for summary judgment denied; summary judgment granted to plaintiff as to liability; matter remanded for an assessment of damages.


HEADNOTES

Consumer Protection -- Telephone Consumer Protection Act 

1. In an action pursuant to the Telephone Consumer Protection Act (TCPA) alleging that defendants transmitted unsolicited advertisements to plaintiff's facsimile (fax) machine without authorization, the fax at issue fell within the TCPA's definition of an "unsolicited advertisement" (47 USC § 227 [b] [3]). The fax included a "strong buy" recommendation for the stock described therein, indicated that defendant recommended the stock, gave the stock's price, "Reasons To Own The Stock" and a contact number. The fax also gave subscription information for future issues of the "newsletter." It therefore proposed a commercial transaction for the stock, the newsletter, or both, and fell within the ambit of the statute. 

Consumer Protection -- Telephone Consumer Protection Act 

2. In an action pursuant to the Telephone Consumer Protection Act (TCPA) alleging that defendants transmitted unsolicited advertisements to plaintiff's facsimile (fax) machine without authorization, the fax at issue fell within the TCPA's definition of an "unsolicited advertisement" (47 USC § 227 [b] [3]). The fax had the effect and purpose of advertising, albeit indirectly, defendant's services. Its essential purpose was to propose a commercial transaction. It mentioned defendant's company name, connecting defendant to the contents of the fax, and invited calls for further information. The fax pitched a product or service under the guise of providing information about it, and therefore proposed a commercial transaction, notwithstanding that the proposal in so many words would occur when the fax recipient called the sender.

Consumer Protection -- Telephone Consumer Protection Act 

3. In determining whether a facsimile (fax) transmitted to a plaintiff's fax machine without authorization falls within the definition of an "unsolicited advertisement" under the Telephone Consumer Protection Act (TCPA) (47 USC § 227 [b] [3]), the court's scrutiny is not limited to the four corners of the fax. Rather, such factors as who the sender is, as well as his motives, purposes and intentions for sending the fax, are all relevant. It is possible that faxes textually conveying the same ideas or even faxes that are facially identical could fall inside or outside the TCPA's ambit depending upon considerations outside the content of the fax itself.

Courts -- New York City Civil Court -- Injunctions 

4. In a New York City Civil Court action pursuant to the Telephone Consumer Protection Act (TCPA), plaintiff was not entitled to equitable relief in the form of an injunction barring defendant from sending further faxes. The remedies available pursuant to the TCPA are limited to those "otherwise permitted by the laws or rules of court of a State" (47 USC § 227 [b] [3]), and plaintiff's forum, the New York City Civil Court, does not have equity jurisdiction to grant, as a form of primary relief, the requested injunction (CCA 202).

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Telephone Consumer Protection Act 

5. The Telephone Consumer Protection Act (TCPA) (47 USC § 227) is constitutional notwithstanding that, in proscribing the use of any telephone facsimile (fax) machine, computer, or other device to send an unsolicited advertisement to a telephone facsimile machine, it restricts commercial speech. The TCPA does not ban any advertising content at all. It simply forbids its transmission by fax to an unwilling recipient, while leaving open to the advertiser all other means of conveying the information. Furthermore, the evils the TCPA seeks to address, shifting of advertising costs to recipients and depriving them of use of their own equipment, are brought about by the act of the fax transmission itself, not by some posited remote result of that transmission. The TCPA restrictions upon transmission of unsolicited advertisements by facsimile are constitutional, since the statute addresses a substantial government interest, materially advances that interest, and is not more extensive than necessary to serve that interest.

People v. O'Connell


NO. 2003-1111 S CR


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM


4 Misc. 3d 54; 781 N.Y.S.2d 400; 2004 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 737



March 9, 2004, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the District Court affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Ordinance Proscribing Noise Disturbance from Barking Dog 

Defendant was properly convicted of violating Town Code of the Town of Smithtown § 207-2 (B) (2) for allowing her dogs to bark for more than 15 minutes cumulatively in the space of an hour. The noise ordinance was not unconstitutionally vague as applied to defendant. It was clearly intended to address excessive noise of canine origin, and defines the noise intensity and duration involved, stating that "a 'noise disturbance from a barking dog' shall be defined as that created by a dog barking for fifteen (15) minutes in any one-hour interval." The statute further defines a "noise disturbance," in part, as a sound that "[d]isturbs a reasonable person of normal sensitivities" (id. § 207-1). To accept defendant's proffered construction of "barking" to exclude the "howling" or "yelping" noises that the complainant testified defendant's dogs were making would introduce into the statute the very vagueness infirmity of which defendant complained. A reasonable person can understand that whatever sound their breed of dog customarily makes that meets the other elements of the ordinance constitutes "a noise disturbance from a barking dog."

People v. Goldstein


NO. 2002-754 K CR


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM


196 Misc. 2d 741; 763 N.Y.S.2d 390; 2003 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 980



July 17, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected August 18, 2003.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed and matter remanded.


HEADNOTES

Crimes--Argument and Conduct of Counsel--Prosecutor's Remarks in Summation

1. Defendant's conviction must be reversed because the prosecutor's remarks in summation exceeded the bounds of propriety and fair response to the defense, and the cumulative effect of these remarks deprived defendant of a fair trial. The prosecutor, without any attempt to temper his language, improperly stated on a number of occasions that defendant had lied about various matters. He also vouched for the complainant on several occasions, and certain of his remarks went beyond any permissible and fair argument that the complainant had testified truthfully. Moreover, the prosecutor improperly denigrated defense counsel on a number of occasions, and ended his summation with gratuitous remarks that had no other purpose but to inflame the jury.

Constitutional Law--Validity of Statute--Aggravated Harassment

2. The second degree aggravated harassment statute (Penal Law § 240.30 [1], [2]) is not unconstitutional as applied to defendant, who is charged with making threatening and harassing telephone calls to the complainant, a former employee, and with mailing to her copies of his adult publication's editorials and adult-content television show segments. The statute does not prohibit speech or expression, and the publication of defendant's remarks in his magazine and the making of them on the air on his show formed no part of the prosecution. Defendant mailed the editorial material directly to complainant, along with a threatening letter, and left her threatening phone messages at home. The statute is properly directed to communications, made, as here, to an unwilling recipient, wherein substantial privacy interests are being invaded in an essentially intolerable manner.

Crimes--Harassment--Aggravated Harassment--Sufficiency of Evidence

3. In a prosecution for second degree harassment and aggravated second degree harassment where defendant is charged with making threatening and harassing telephone calls to the complainant, a former employee, and with mailing to her copies of his adult publication's editorials and adult-content television show segments, the People introduced admissible evidence from which a rational jury could find that defendant made each of the communications underlying each of the counts of which he was convicted either in a manner likely to annoy or alarm, or without any legitimate purpose of communication, and that he did so with the requisite intent. A jury could properly find that defendant used abusive and profane language extensively in all the communications at issue, knowing complainant's sensitivity to such language, and that various portions of the language constituted threats, indicating both intent and that the complainant was an unwilling recipient. A jury could also properly find, in the case of the phone calls, that they did not attempt to discuss any legitimate subject matter, and from this, that they were made with no legitimate purpose of communication. In the case of the letter accompanying the editorials, the jury was entitled to find that the letter contained threats and, furthermore, that the communication was made in a manner likely to annoy or alarm.

Crimes--Defendant as Witness--Impeachment--Published Statements

4. The prosecution properly used editorial statements published in defendant's magazine, to the effect that terrorists should fly airplanes into and send anthrax letters to the District Attorney's office, in cross-examining defendant. While a pretrial Sandoval ruling had forbidden use of those statements to impeach defendant's credibility, defendant subsequently opened the door to their use by placing his character for peacefulness at issue. It is not the content of defendant's words, but their potential for provocation to violence, that is relevant to the prosecution. Defendant's words were, self-admittedly, meant to be inflammatory when made.

People v. DeRojas

May 15, 2003, Decided


HEADNOTES

Motor Vehicles - Traffic Infractions - Constitutionality of Simplified Traffic Information for Misdemeanor Charge of Driving while Intoxicated

It is not unconstitutional for traffic infractions, including defendant's misdemeanor charge of driving while intoxicated, to be charged either by simplified traffic information or misdemeanor information, both of which have different requirements for facial sufficiency (CPL 100.15, 100.25, 100.40). The simplified traffic information, a statutory creation designed to provide an uncomplicated form for handling a large volume of traffic infractions and petty offenses, has a rational basis and has been sanctioned for use with traffic misdemeanors.

795 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Weinkselbaum


NO. 2001-572 S CR


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, SECOND DEPARTMENT


194 Misc. 2d 19; 753 N.Y.S.2d 284; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1537



July 2, 2002, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law - Freedom of Speech - Regulation of Signs

The Town of Babylon's fee-based, temporary sign ordinance, which requires a permit to erect a noncommercial sign in a residential district subject to a 30-day, nonrenewable expiration period, is not facially unconstitutional for First Amendment purposes. The ordinance is sufficiently "tailored" to satisfy constitutional requirements. The ordinance permits noncommercial speech in both commercial and residential zones without regard to content, and there are no content-based limitations on time or exemptions from the fee. The temporary permits, available on demand for use in "all districts" for a nominal fee, provide an "ample" alternative channel for communication. The nominal permit fees and 30-day time limitation are permissible burdens incidental to the Town's legitimate and substantial interest in sign regulation. Moreover, the ordinance's "advance notice" requirement does not imply an impermissible prior restraint as to protected content, in view of the absence of discretionary authority and content-based guidelines. Furthermore, defendant's equal protection challenge to the ordinance cannot be determined because of his failure to seek a permit. Accordingly, defendant's conviction of erecting a sign critical of local law enforcement practices on the lawn of his home without the requisite permit is affirmed.

People v. Mangano

HEADNOTES

Crimes - Aggravated Harassment - Telephone Messages to Public Officials' Workplace

Defendant was lawfully convicted of aggravated harassment in the second degree (Penal Law § 240.30 [1]) for having left messages on complainants' workplace telephone answering machine, containing threats to them and their families, profanely abusive language, and thinly-veiled references to retaliation for the perceived nonperformance of their official duties. The statute, in proscribing the use of a telephone to communicate in a manner likely to cause annoyance or alarm, is neither unconstitutional on its face nor as applied to the facts herein. Government employees retain substantial privacy interests at their official workplaces, which may be protected from invasion in an essentially intolerable manner.

People v. Stuart


NY County Clerk's # 571011-00


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, FIRST DEPARTMENT


191 Misc. 2d 541; 742 N.Y.S.2d 767; 2002 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 391



April 30, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected May 21, 2002. Counsel and Opinion Corrected June 4, 2002.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law - Validity of Statute - Stalking Statute Not Void for Vagueness

Defendant was lawfully convicted of stalking in the fourth degree ( Penal Law § 120.45) where the evidence established that defendant, after offering complainant unsolicited gifts during a street encounter, followed her every day for more than five weeks, making her feel "very scared" and "very uncomfortable." The statute is not unconstitutionally vague. The statute makes it unlawful to "intentionally, and for no legitimate purpose, engage[] in a course of conduct directed at a specific person" that would likely instill reasonable fear of material physical harm in the targeted person or that actually causes material mental or emotional harm to the targeted person, where the violator knows or reasonably should know that such conduct would elicit the requisite fear or cause the requisite harm. The stalking statute thus provides sufficient notice of the conduct proscribed and is written in a manner that does not encourage arbitrary or discriminatory enforcement. The term "legitimate purpose," when read in conjunction with the rest of the statutory language, including the requirement that the course of conduct be undertaken "intentionally," conveys sufficiently definite warning as to the proscribed conduct when measured by common understanding and practices. Furthermore, the statute's lack of a specific intent component does not render the statute void for vagueness.

Jones v. Allen


NO. 99-1493 K C


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE TERM, SECOND DEPARTMENT


185 Misc. 2d 443; 712 N.Y.S.2d 306; 2000 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 317



June 27, 2000, Decided


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law - Validity of Statute - Stay in Summary Proceeding Conditioned upon Deposit of Judgment

 RPAPL 747-a, which, in nonpayment proceedings brought in the City of New York, bars the granting of stays after five days have elapsed following the entry of judgment unless the amount of the judgment has either been paid to the landlord or deposited into court, is unconstitutional as applied to temporary stays in orders to show cause seeking relief from a final judgment or warrant. The statute, as applied to such stays, impermissibly interferes with the court's inherent judicial function. The power to grant such stays is a power which is essential to the just resolution of the summary proceeding, and it is not within the competence of the Legislature to proscribe the granting of such stays unless a mandatory deposit has been made. While it may be true that the need to prevent prejudice to the landlord ordinarily dictates that a stay be conditioned upon a deposit of the judgment amount, there are circumstances in which a deposit of the full amount should be dispensed with. Accordingly, respondent tenant was properly granted a temporary stay of the warrant of eviction without posting a deposit of the judgment amount, particularly in light of the long duration of the tenancy, where the Department of Social Services (DSS) checks she had to pay the judgment with could not be deposited because they were not made out to the proper party, and the lateness in payment was due to a delay in receiving funds from DSS.

Cole v. Town of Esopus


1158-16


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, ALBANY COUNTY


55 Misc. 3d 382; 47 N.Y.S.3d 634; 2016 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4935; 2016 NY Slip Op 26452



 December 13, 2016, Decided


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Proposed Sober Living Residence Did Not Constitute One-Family Dwelling

1. Respondent town zoning board of appeals' determination that a proposed sober living residence was not a one-family dwelling under the town zoning code was not arbitrary and capricious, given the size and physical layout of the residence, the services and treatments to be provided, and the transience of the average resident. The proposed residence consisted of 7,300 square feet of space, with at least eight bedrooms, a dormitory-style bathroom, common areas, rooms for offices, counseling and treatment activities, and a parking lot for 12 cars. It would be licensed and regulated by the Office of Alcoholism and Substance Abuse Services (OASAS), and petitioner was required, pursuant to its contract with OASAS and regulations, to provide around-the-clock services and supervision. As such, the operational dynamics of the residence were distinguished from the common one-family dwelling. The purpose of the residence was to provide transitional support from chemical dependency treatment programs into the community, and the adult residents would have had no prior relationships with each other, and stay at the residence for an average of five to eight months. Thus, the proposed residence was unlike a group home with unrelated residents living interdependently in relatively stable relationships and sharing household activities and responsibilities as the functional equivalent of a family.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Proposed Sober Living Residence Did Not Constitute Convalescent Home

2. Respondent town zoning board of appeals' determination that a proposed sober living residence in the nature of an assisted living residential facility was a convalescent home under the town zoning code was arbitrary and capricious since there was no evidence that petitioner would be providing nursing care to the residents. While credentialed professional and paraprofessional staff would be required to provide services to the residents, those services would entail supervision, substance abuse and other supportive counseling, assistance in securing out-patient medical services and other services, vocational services, and transportation to and from appointments. The residents would already have undergone intensive medical treatment for substance abuse at a different facility before being transferred to the proposed transitional facility. Moreover, while the proposed facility would include a "meds room," and staff would dispense previously prescribed medications to the residents as part of their effort to help residents develop a routine that would allow them to function independently in the community, nursing staff was not required to dispense such medications.

Civil Rights -- Discrimination in Housing -- Proposed Sober Living Residence

3. Respondent town zoning board of appeals' determination that a proposed sober living residence was not a one-family dwelling under the town zoning code was not violative of federal anti-discrimination laws based on a disproportionate impact on individuals in addiction recovery. The proposed residence would be neither self-governed nor self-supporting, trained staff would supervise the residents around the clock, engage in counseling, and distribute medications, and the duration of residency was expected to be transient, on the order of months. Moreover, other zoning districts within the town permitted the same type of facility as of right or by special permit.

 

800 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v Washington


2016KN026813


CRIMINAL COURT OF THE CITY OF NEW YORK, KINGS COUNTY


54 Misc. 3d 802; 42 N.Y.S.3d 604; 2016 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4457; 2016 NY Slip Op 26401



 December 2, 2016, Decided


HEADNOTES

Local Laws -- Validity -- Local Regulation of Traffic and Pedestrians

1. Administrative Code of the City of New York § 19-190, which imposes criminal liability on "any driver of a motor vehicle who [fails to yield to a pedestrian who has the right of way] and thereby causes physical injury" to the pedestrian (Administrative Code § 19-190 [b]), is not rendered unconstitutional by the exclusion in section 19-190 (c) for incidents in which "the failure to yield and/or physical injury was not caused by the driver's failure to exercise due care." The exclusion is precise, limited and fully contained within the statute, and therefore reflects the New York City Council's intent that the failure to exercise "due care" is an element of the crime. While the use of a civil negligence standard is inappropriate in a criminal prosecution, there is no basis to conclude, solely upon the use of the words "due care," that the Council meant to drastically change the historical distinction between criminal and civil liability. Thus, the use of the term "due care" incorporates the criminal negligence mens rea of Penal Law § 15.05 (4), which requires the finder of fact to look to the defendant's own awareness of the risk.

Local Laws -- Preemption by State -- Failure to Yield Right of Way -- Mental Culpability

2. Administrative Code of the City of New York § 19-190 (b), which imposes criminal liability on "any driver of a motor vehicle who [fails to yield to a pedestrian who has the right of way] and thereby causes physical injury" to the pedestrian, is not preempted by Penal Law article 15. Penal Law § 15.15 (2) mandates that, unless a statute defining a crime clearly indicates a legislative intent to impose strict liability, the statute is to be construed to be a crime of "mental culpability." As Administrative Code § 19-190 (b) is not clearly designated as a crime of strict liability, Penal Law § 15.15 (2) requires the "due care" language of Administrative Code § 19-190 (c), which excludes from criminal liability incidents in which "the failure to yield and/or physical injury was not caused by the driver's failure to exercise due care," to be read as one of the Penal Law's culpable mental states in order to give effect to the intention of the New York City Council. Thus, reading the definition of criminal negligence in Penal Law § 15.05 (4) into Administrative Code § 19-190 does not conflict with state law.

Local Laws -- Preemption by State -- Local Regulation of Traffic and Pedestrians

3. Administrative Code of the City of New York § 19-190 (b), which imposes criminal liability on "any driver of a motor vehicle who [fails to yield to a pedestrian who has the right of way] and thereby causes physical injury" to the pedestrian, is not preempted by state law regulating a driver's duty to yield to pedestrians who have the right of way (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1146). The City of New York has the power to pass legislation regulating the safety of its citizens so long as the local law is not inconsistent with any state law already governing the same conduct and does not seek to occupy a field of regulation for which the State has assumed full regulatory responsibility. Moreover, Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1642 (a) provides the City of New York with authority to pass laws "regulat[ing] traffic on or pedestrian use of any highway" in the City, and that such laws "shall supersede the provisions of [the Vehicle and Traffic Law] where inconsistent or in conflict with respect to the ... [r]ight of way of vehicles and pedestrians" (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1642 [a] [10]). Thus, even were Administrative Code § 19-190 (b) in conflict with the Vehicle and Traffic Law, it would supersede the state law in this area.

Crimes -- Consolidation and Severance -- Same Criminal Transaction

4. In a prosecution in which crack cocaine was recovered from defendant's pocket following an accident allegedly caused by defendant's failure to yield the right of way to a pedestrian, the charge of criminal possession of a controlled substance was properly joined with the other charges in the accusatory instrument because it was part of the same criminal transaction. CPL 200.20 (2) (a) provides that "[t]wo offenses are 'joinable' when . . . [t]hey are based upon the same act or upon the same criminal transaction, as that term is defined in [CPL 40.10 (2)]." Defendant was alleged to have been in possession of crack cocaine while operating his motor vehicle, and the drug possession charge was thus so closely related in time, place and date to the other charges to have been part of the same criminal transaction.
